I should add that I\u2019m not talking about the silly soundscape used in cartoons and other trivia, which centres on a note transcribed as <em>ribittt</em>: common frogs never make that noise.</p> <p>Their music is a soft, continuous purr that, for all its quietness, has an extraordinary impact. And it\u2019s not always quiet. A friend remarked recently that frogs near him sounded \u201clike a Harley Davison rally\u201d. He exaggerates, but I know what he means: there\u2019s a touch of the distant motorbike in many pond choruses.</p> <p>I think of it more as a kind of infrasound, perhaps like the rumbling made by elephants that carries over great distances, or as something subterranean, as if all the sap and juices of an awakening Earth could find a way to express their seasonal uprising. The common frog\u2019s voice is emblematic of it all.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"7ee99394e86ea3bca6b485458cdb8941046eb941\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/7ee99394e86ea3bca6b485458cdb8941046eb941/0_187_3627_2177/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Frogs in a pond with eyes like their spawn\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Their heads rested at the surface, forelimbs arced before them.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Mark Cocker</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Having declared its capacity to move me, I should add instantly that watching the live performance involves entirely different emotions. George Orwell said that toads reminded him of \u201cstrict Anglo-Catholics towards the end of Lent\u201d, and frogs have a similarly serious demeanour. In my recent encounter, a cluster of males eventually permitted me to sit in attendance while they sang, albeit watching me in a circlet of disapproving caution around a mass of spawn.</p> <p>Their heads rested at the surface, forelimbs arced before them and their remarkable three-tone eyes looking exactly like a black-flecked ball of spawns. Yet it was the voluminous white throat that held all my attention.</p> <p>As each male adds his ha\u2019p\u2019orth to the flowing reel of their free-form chorus, the throat balloons out to twice the width of the head. In this momentary peak it resembles an almost luminous bubble, and it led me to appreciate how frogsong is often a nocturnal affair, when all the human eye would see is these globes of white bellowing in the dark. Then, the climax having been achieved, each frog deflates, its mouth shrinks, the face puckers up, reminding me ever so slightly of my beloved grandmother in her late eighties.</p> <p>\u2022 Country diary is on Twitter at <a href=\"https://twitter.com/gdncountrydiary?lang=en\" title=\"\">@gdncountrydiary</a></p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "9", "wordcount": "374", "commentCloseDate": "2023-04-07T04:30:36Z", "commentable": "true", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T04:30:36Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "true", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T04:30:36Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-04T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Guardian", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/njjd3", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/17ed9e33e7f5a68e3cef7c3ef4b36e14f999324c/0_0_3007_1804/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "I\u2019m wondering if frogsong is the most wonderful sound in British nature that is now the least known and certainly the most underrated. I should add that I\u2019m not talking about the silly soundscape used in cartoons and other trivia, which centres on a note transcribed as ribittt: common frogs never make that noise. Their music is a soft, continuous purr that, for all its quietness, has an extraordinary impact. And it\u2019s not always quiet. A friend remarked recently that frogs near him sounded \u201clike a Harley Davison rally\u201d. He exaggerates, but I know what he means: there\u2019s a touch of the distant motorbike in many pond choruses. I think of it more as a kind of infrasound, perhaps like the rumbling made by elephants that carries over great distances, or as something subterranean, as if all the sap and juices of an awakening Earth could find a way to express their seasonal uprising. The common frog\u2019s voice is emblematic of it all. Having declared its capacity to move me, I should add instantly that watching the live performance involves entirely different emotions. George Orwell said that toads reminded him of \u201cstrict Anglo-Catholics towards the end of Lent\u201d, and frogs have a similarly serious demeanour. In my recent encounter, a cluster of males eventually permitted me to sit in attendance while they sang, albeit watching me in a circlet of disapproving caution around a mass of spawn. Their heads rested at the surface, forelimbs arced before them and their remarkable three-tone eyes looking exactly like a black-flecked ball of spawns. Yet it was the voluminous white throat that held all my attention. As each male adds his ha\u2019p\u2019orth to the flowing reel of their free-form chorus, the throat balloons out to twice the width of the head. In this momentary peak it resembles an almost luminous bubble, and it led me to appreciate how frogsong is often a nocturnal affair, when all the human eye would see is these globes of white bellowing in the dark. class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Leon Neal/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>In an anonymous muddy field somewhere in Ukraine, the country\u2019s defence minister crouched in the commander\u2019s hatch of a British Challenger 2 tank, one of the first three of 14 to be delivered to Kyiv.</p> <p>In a maroon jacket and matching baseball cap, Oleksii Reznikov gave a thumbs-up as he thanked the UK for the delivery of the system.</p> <p>It is not only <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/27/ukrainian-troops-return-home-after-challenger-2-tank-training-in-uk\">Challenger main battle tanks</a> that are arriving. In recent weeks a steady flow of donated armour has been arriving in Ukraine. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/28/german-leopard-2-tanks-now-in-ukraine-berlin-confirms\">German Leopard 2 tanks</a> and Marder infantry fighting vehicles, US Strykers and Cougars, scores in number, have been shipped discretely, with more to follow.</p> <p>The sudden influx of new armour, as well as the accelerated training of Ukrainian crews to operate the vehicles in the UK and elsewhere, comes ahead of the expectation \u2013 raised by senior Ukrainian officials \u2013 that they may soon be in action as part of the country\u2019s anticipated spring offensive.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/challenger2_tank/giv-6562tg1Ivn52s0iS/\" data-alt=\"Graphic of Challenger 2 tank\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/challenger2_tank/giv-6562tg1Ivn52s0iS/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>And while over the winter Ukraine\u2019s president, Volodmyr Zelenskiy, had been pressing for 300 main battle tanks as well as other vehicles and modern fighter jets, the much more modest numbers that have so far arrived is fuelling speculation over how that armour may be used in the coming weeks and months.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/02/surrounded-and-outgunned-ukraines-tank-crews-prepare-for-battle-of-bakhmut\">Surrounded and outgunned, Ukraine\u2019s tank crews prepare for battle of Bakhmut</a> </p> </aside>  <p>With Russia\u2019s own recent offensive in the eastern Donbas region apparently petering out with minimal gains and heavy losses, not least around the key <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/02/surrounded-and-outgunned-ukraines-tank-crews-prepare-for-battle-of-bakhmut\">eastern city of Bakhmut</a>, there is little expectation that Ukraine has the resources for the kind of large-scale armoured thrust that Russia itself failed to carry off at the beginning of its invasion of Ukraine.</p> <p>Instead, analysts \u2013 including former British tank commanders \u2013 anticipate a counteroffensive, or counteroffensives, similar to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/sep/10/ukrainian-forces-kupiansk-advances-north-east-russia-izyum\">those that took large amounts of territory from Russia</a> in the late summer and autumn around Kharkiv oblast and in the country\u2019s south that led to the retaking of Kherson.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5dc4bfac9fbd267f6a260034372b0793814c7c0d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5dc4bfac9fbd267f6a260034372b0793814c7c0d/0_408_6123_3673/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A Javelin missile is fired from a US Stryker infantry carrier vehicle in Fort Carson, Colorado\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A Javelin missile is fired from a US Stryker infantry carrier vehicle in Fort Carson, Colorado.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Michael Ciaglo/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>And while they point to the south as the most likely theatre for a spring counteroffensive, they caution that Ukraine\u2019s effective employment of misinformation and deception in last year\u2019s fighting make feints and so-called demonstrations likely.</p> <p>Among those who have been considering what the new armour arriving in Ukraine may mean for the war is Hamish de Bretton-Gordon, who served as a tank commander with 14th/20th King\u2019s Hussars in the first Gulf war before going on to command the UK\u2019s Joint Chemical, Biological, Radiological and Nuclear Regiment.</p> <p>For De Bretton-Gordon, one of the most significant aspects of the accumulating western armour in Ukraine is the psychological aspect.</p> <p>\u201cPsychologically, it is hugely positive for the Ukrainians and negative for the Russians. If we are just talking about numbers, then on the face of it 50 to 60 modern western tanks in Ukraine is not necessarily a gamechanger.</p> <p>\u201cI\u2019ve said in the past that you would need a minimum of 100 for a big punch, and Ukraine has been talking about needing 300.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/leopard2a6-zip/giv-16266AQ14LNhPNvmt/\" data-alt=\"Graphic of Leopard tank\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/leopard2a6-zip/giv-16266AQ14LNhPNvmt/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Despite that, De Bretton-Gordon believes the superior capabilities the tanks offer in comparison with most Russian main battle tanks that have been deployed in Ukraine, and the more effective Ukrainian tactics, will make a difference.</p> <p>\u201cLet\u2019s not forget that these tanks are in addition to the [Soviet and post-Soviet era] T72 tanks Ukraine already has, and they are arriving for a military that has been well trained in manoeuvre warfare and at a time when it is very clear that the Russians are incapable of anything but frontal attacks.\u201d</p> <p>In particular, De Bretton-Gordon says, the ability of Leopards to stand off and fire at greater range, and superior armour in comparison with many of the tanks being fielded by Moscow, may give them an edge. They also have a better ability to operate cross-country in comparison with the lower-clearance Russian tanks and fight effectively at night.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"fc2ae6ac6ce78707e334927f0213d98303724dac\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/fc2ae6ac6ce78707e334927f0213d98303724dac/281_760_7907_4744/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A Ukrainian tank in the frontline area near  Bakhmut, Ukraine\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A Ukrainian tank in the frontline area near Bakhmut. The new tanks are superior to those currently used.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Anadolu Agency/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>With the relatively small numbers available, the question is to what use they will be put.</p> <p>\u201cArmour works best in a shock action breaking [enemy lines],\u201d says De Bretton-Gordon. \u201cThey will want to concentrate firepower rather than disperse it.</p> <p>\u201cThat\u2019s where I think western armour comes in with the rest of the Ukrainian armour following. What feels most logical is something more limited, like breaking the connection between Russian-occupied parts of the Donbas and south.</p> <p>\u201cI can see them wanting to create a widening gap and dislocating the Russians by driving a couple hundred-mile route through that area [south and east of Zaporizhzhia].\u201d</p> <p>Stuart Crawford, who served as an officer in the 4th Royal Tank Regiment, also believes while there are not enough tanks and other western armoured vehicles arriving in Ukraine to make a dramatic difference, they may be useful in a more limited counteroffensive, again pointing towards the south.</p> <p>\u201cIt can\u2019t be the classic punch-a-hole followed by an expanding torrent approach. It\u2019s going to be more localised. And if you are looking at it from a strategic point of view the clear thing that has been talked about is an offensive south from Zaporizhzhia towards Melitopol.\u201d</p> <p>He adds: \u201cThe quality of the tanks arriving is superior to most of the stuff of Russian and Soviet/post-Soviet origin currently being used on both sides.\u201d</p> <p>Crawford is also sceptical of suggestions that having so many different weapons systems coming from different sources will necessarily cause maintenance and logistical problems. \u201cIf you look at the experience of the allies in Normandy in 1944 they had a vast array of equipment from different sources, and still managed.</p> <p>\u201cBut logistically, it probably makes sense to put all the Leopards into the same organisation.\u201d</p> <p>Where Crawford does see a gap, however, is in the potential lack of air support. Neither side has achieved overall air superiority, which is why the Ukrainians have been so <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/19/us-f-16s-ukraine-un-ambassador\">vocal in asking for US F16 jets</a>, which are widely available, because without air superiority ground forces cannot move easily.</p> <p>As well as the tanks, Crawford sees the arrival of armoured infantry carriers such as the US Strykers as also being highly useful on a \u201cbattlefield so dominated by artillery\u201d.</p> <p>What does seem clear from Ukrainian statements, and Russian defensive preparations, is that whatever Ukraine is planning to do with its new weapons will be revealed sooner rather than later.</p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "24", "wordcount": "1030", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T04:00:36Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T20:28:59Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-05T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Guardian", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nkn47", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/c62f5fa92cc759f787026fcaabf9bc82d0c24d54/0_363_7955_4773/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "In an anonymous muddy field somewhere in Ukraine, the country\u2019s defence minister crouched in the commander\u2019s hatch of a British Challenger 2 tank, one of the first three of 14 to be delivered to Kyiv. In a maroon jacket and matching baseball cap, Oleksii Reznikov gave a thumbs-up as he thanked the UK for the delivery of the system. It is not only Challenger main battle tanks that are arriving. In recent weeks a steady flow of donated armour has been arriving in Ukraine. German Leopard 2 tanks and Marder infantry fighting vehicles, US Strykers and Cougars, scores in number, have been shipped discretely, with more to follow. The sudden influx of new armour, as well as the accelerated training of Ukrainian crews to operate the vehicles in the UK and elsewhere, comes ahead of the expectation \u2013 raised by senior Ukrainian officials \u2013 that they may soon be in action as part of the country\u2019s anticipated spring offensive. And while over the winter Ukraine\u2019s president, Volodmyr Zelenskiy, had been pressing for 300 main battle tanks as well as other vehicles and modern fighter jets, the much more modest numbers that have so far arrived is fuelling speculation over how that armour may be used in the coming weeks and months. With Russia\u2019s own recent offensive in the eastern Donbas region apparently petering out with minimal gains and heavy losses, not least around the key eastern city of Bakhmut, there is little expectation that Ukraine has the resources for the kind of large-scale armoured thrust that Russia itself failed to carry off at the beginning of its invasion of Ukraine. Instead, analysts \u2013 including former British tank commanders \u2013 anticipate a counteroffensive, or counteroffensives, similar to those that took large amounts of territory from Russia in the late summer and autumn around Kharkiv oblast and in the country\u2019s south that led to the retaking of Kherson. And while they point to the south as the most likely theatre for a spring counteroffensive, they caution that Ukraine\u2019s effective employment of misinformation and deception in last year\u2019s fighting make feints and so-called demonstrations likely. Among those who have been considering what the new armour arriving in Ukraine may mean for the war is Hamish de Bretton-Gordon, who served as a tank commander with 14th/20th King\u2019s Hussars in the first Gulf war before going on to command the UK\u2019s Joint Chemical, Biological, Radiological and Nuclear Regiment. For De Bretton-Gordon, one of the most significant aspects of the accumulating western armour in Ukraine is the psychological aspect. \u201cPsychologically, it is hugely positive for the Ukrainians and negative for the Russians. If we are just talking about numbers, then on the face of it 50 to 60 modern western tanks in Ukraine is not necessarily a gamechanger. \u201cI\u2019ve said in the past that you would need a minimum of 100 for a big punch, and Ukraine has been talking about needing 300.\u201d Despite that, De Bretton-Gordon believes the superior capabilities the tanks offer in comparison with most Russian main battle tanks that have been deployed in Ukraine, and the more effective Ukrainian tactics, will make a difference. \u201cLet\u2019s not forget that these tanks are in addition to the [Soviet and post-Soviet era] T72 tanks Ukraine already has, and they are arriving for a military that has been well trained in manoeuvre warfare and at a time when it is very clear that the Russians are incapable of anything but frontal attacks.\u201d In particular, De Bretton-Gordon says, the ability of Leopards to stand off and fire at greater range, and superior armour in comparison with many of the tanks being fielded by Moscow, may give them an edge. They also have a better ability to operate cross-country in comparison with the lower-clearance Russian tanks and fight effectively at night. With the relatively small numbers available, the question is to what use they will be put. \u201cArmour works best in a shock action breaking [enemy lines],\u201d says De Bretton-Gordon. \u201cThey will want to concentrate firepower rather than disperse it. \u201cThat\u2019s where I think western armour comes in with the rest of the Ukrainian armour following. What feels most logical is something more limited, like breaking the connection between Russian-occupied parts of the Donbas and south. \u201cI can see them wanting to create a widening gap and dislocating the Russians by driving a couple hundred-mile route through that area [south and east of Zaporizhzhia].\u201d Stuart Crawford, who served as an officer in the 4th Royal Tank Regiment, also believes while there are not enough tanks and other western armoured vehicles arriving in Ukraine to make a dramatic difference, they may be useful in a more limited counteroffensive, again pointing towards the south. \u201cIt can\u2019t be the classic punch-a-hole followed by an expanding torrent approach. It\u2019s going to be more localised. And if you are looking at it from a strategic point of view the clear thing that has been talked about is an offensive south from Zaporizhzhia towards Melitopol.\u201d He adds: \u201cThe quality of the tanks arriving is superior to most of the stuff of Russian and Soviet/post-Soviet origin currently being used on both sides.\u201d Crawford is also sceptical of suggestions that having so many different weapons systems coming from different sources will necessarily cause maintenance and logistical problems. \u201cIf you look at the experience of the allies in Normandy in 1944 they had a vast array of equipment from different sources, and still managed. \u201cBut logistically, it probably makes sense to put all the Leopards into the same organisation.\u201d Where Crawford does see a gap, however, is in the potential lack of air support. Neither side has achieved overall air superiority, which is why the Ukrainians have been so vocal in asking for US F16 jets, which are widely available, because without air superiority ground forces cannot move easily. As well as the tanks, Crawford sees the arrival of armoured infantry carriers such as the US Strykers as also being highly useful on a \u201cbattlefield so dominated by artillery\u201d. What does seem clear from Ukrainian statements, and Russian defensive preparations, is that whatever Ukraine is planning to do with its new weapons will be revealed sooner rather than later.", as the accelerated training of Ukrainian crews to operate the vehicles in the UK and elsewhere, comes ahead of the expectation \u2013 raised by senior Ukrainian officials \u2013 that they may soon be in action as part of the country\u2019s anticipated spring offensive.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/challenger2_tank/giv-6562tg1Ivn52s0iS/\" data-alt=\"Graphic of Challenger 2 tank\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/challenger2_tank/giv-6562tg1Ivn52s0iS/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>And while over the winter Ukraine\u2019s president, Volodmyr Zelenskiy, had been pressing for 300 main battle tanks as well as other vehicles and modern fighter jets, the much more modest numbers that have so far arrived is fuelling speculation over how that armour may be used in the coming weeks and months.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/02/surrounded-and-outgunned-ukraines-tank-crews-prepare-for-battle-of-bakhmut\">Surrounded and outgunned, Ukraine\u2019s tank crews prepare for battle of Bakhmut</a> </p> </aside>  <p>With Russia\u2019s own recent offensive in the eastern Donbas region apparently petering out with minimal gains and heavy losses, not least around the key <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/02/surrounded-and-outgunned-ukraines-tank-crews-prepare-for-battle-of-bakhmut\">eastern city of Bakhmut</a>, there is little expectation that Ukraine has the resources for the kind of large-scale armoured thrust that Russia itself failed to carry off at the beginning of its invasion of Ukraine.</p> <p>Instead, analysts \u2013 including former British tank commanders \u2013 anticipate a counteroffensive, or counteroffensives, similar to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/sep/10/ukrainian-forces-kupiansk-advances-north-east-russia-izyum\">those that took large amounts of territory from Russia</a> in the late summer and autumn around Kharkiv oblast and in the country\u2019s south that led to the retaking of Kherson.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5dc4bfac9fbd267f6a260034372b0793814c7c0d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5dc4bfac9fbd267f6a260034372b0793814c7c0d/0_408_6123_3673/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A Javelin missile is fired from a US Stryker infantry carrier vehicle in Fort Carson, Colorado\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A Javelin missile is fired from a US Stryker infantry carrier vehicle in Fort Carson, Colorado.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Michael Ciaglo/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>And while they point to the south as the most likely theatre for a spring counteroffensive, they caution that Ukraine\u2019s effective employment of misinformation and deception in last year\u2019s fighting make feints and so-called demonstrations likely.</p> <p>Among those who have been considering what the new armour arriving in Ukraine may mean for the war is Hamish de Bretton-Gordon, who served as a tank commander with 14th/20th King\u2019s Hussars in the first Gulf war before going on to command the UK\u2019s Joint Chemical, Biological, Radiological and Nuclear Regiment.</p> <p>For De Bretton-Gordon, one of the most significant aspects of the accumulating western armour in Ukraine is the psychological aspect.</p> <p>\u201cPsychologically, it is hugely positive for the Ukrainians and negative for the Russians. If we are just talking about numbers, then on the face of it 50 to 60 modern western tanks in Ukraine is not necessarily a gamechanger.</p> <p>\u201cI\u2019ve said in the past that you would need a minimum of 100 for a big punch, and Ukraine has been talking about needing 300.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/leopard2a6-zip/giv-16266AQ14LNhPNvmt/\" data-alt=\"Graphic of Leopard tank\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/01/leopard2a6-zip/giv-16266AQ14LNhPNvmt/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Despite that, De Bretton-Gordon believes the superior capabilities the tanks offer in comparison with most Russian main battle tanks that have been deployed in Ukraine, and the more effective Ukrainian tactics, will make a difference.</p> <p>\u201cLet\u2019s not forget that these tanks are in addition to the [Soviet and post-Soviet era] T72 tanks Ukraine already has, and they are arriving for a military that has been well trained in manoeuvre warfare and at a time when it is very clear that the Russians are incapable of anything but frontal attacks.\u201d</p> <p>In particular, De Bretton-Gordon says, the ability of Leopards to stand off and fire at greater range, and superior armour in comparison with many of the tanks being fielded by Moscow, may give them an edge. They also have a better ability to operate cross-country in comparison with the lower-clearance Russian tanks and fight effectively at night.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"fc2ae6ac6ce78707e334927f0213d98303724dac\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/fc2ae6ac6ce78707e334927f0213d98303724dac/281_760_7907_4744/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A Ukrainian tank in the frontline area near  Bakhmut, Ukraine\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A Ukrainian tank in the frontline area near Bakhmut. The new tanks are superior to those currently used.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Anadolu Agency/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>With the relatively small numbers available, the question is to what use they will be put.</p> <p>\u201cArmour works best in a shock action breaking [enemy lines],\u201d says De Bretton-Gordon. \u201cThey will want to concentrate firepower rather than disperse it.</p> <p>\u201cThat\u2019s where I think western armour comes in with the rest of the Ukrainian armour following. What feels most logical is something more limited, like breaking the connection between Russian-occupied parts of the Donbas and south.</p> <p>\u201cI can see them wanting to create a widening gap and dislocating the Russians by driving a couple hundred-mile route through that area [south and east of Zaporizhzhia].\u201d</p> <p>Stuart Crawford, who served as an officer in the 4th Royal Tank Regiment, also believes while there are not enough tanks and other western armoured vehicles arriving in Ukraine to make a dramatic difference, they may be useful in a more limited counteroffensive, again pointing towards the south.</p> <p>\u201cIt can\u2019t be the classic punch-a-hole followed by an expanding torrent approach. It\u2019s going to be more localised. And if you are looking at it from a strategic point of view the clear thing that has been talked about is an offensive south from Zaporizhzhia towards Melitopol.\u201d</p> <p>He adds: \u201cThe quality of the tanks arriving is superior to most of the stuff of Russian and Soviet/post-Soviet origin currently being used on both sides.\u201d</p> <p>Crawford is also sceptical of suggestions that having so many different weapons systems coming from different sources will necessarily cause maintenance and logistical problems. \u201cIf you look at the experience of the allies in Normandy in 1944 they had a vast array of equipment from different sources, and still managed.</p> <p>\u201cBut logistically, it probably makes sense to put all the Leopards into the same organisation.\u201d</p> <p>Where Crawford does see a gap, however, is in the potential lack of air support. Neither side has achieved overall air superiority, which is why the Ukrainians have been so <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/19/us-f-16s-ukraine-un-ambassador\">vocal in asking for US F16 jets</a>, which are widely available, because without air superiority ground forces cannot move easily.</p> <p>As well as the tanks, Crawford sees the arrival of armoured infantry carriers such as the US Strykers as also being highly useful on a \u201cbattlefield so dominated by artillery\u201d.</p> <p>What does seem clear from Ukrainian statements, and Russian defensive preparations, is that whatever Ukraine is planning to do with its new weapons will be revealed sooner rather than later.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "In an anonymous muddy field somewhere in Ukraine, the country\u2019s defence minister crouched in the commander\u2019s hatch of a British Challenger 2 tank, one of the first three of 14 to be delivered to Kyiv. In a maroon jacket and matching baseball cap, Oleksii Reznikov gave a thumbs-up as he thanked the UK for the delivery of the system. It is not only Challenger main battle tanks that are arriving. In recent weeks a steady flow of donated armour has been arriving in Ukraine. German Leopard 2 tanks and Marder infantry fighting vehicles, US Strykers and Cougars, scores in number, have been shipped discretely, with more to follow. The sudden influx of new armour, as well as the accelerated training of Ukrainian crews to operate the vehicles in the UK and elsewhere, comes ahead of the expectation \u2013 raised by senior Ukrainian officials \u2013 that they may soon be in action as part of the country\u2019s anticipated spring offensive.\nAnd while over the winter Ukraine\u2019s president, Volodmyr Zelenskiy, had been pressing for 300 main battle tanks as well as other vehicles and modern fighter jets, the much more modest numbers that have so far arrived is fuelling speculation over how that armour may be used in the coming weeks and months.\nWith Russia\u2019s own recent offensive in the eastern Donbas region apparently petering out with minimal gains and heavy losses, not least around the key eastern city of Bakhmut, there is little expectation that Ukraine has the resources for the kind of large-scale armoured thrust that Russia itself failed to carry off at the beginning of its invasion of Ukraine. Instead, analysts \u2013 including former British tank commanders \u2013 anticipate a counteroffensive, or counteroffensives, similar to those that took large amounts of territory from Russia in the late summer and autumn around Kharkiv oblast and in the country\u2019s south that led to the retaking of Kherson.\nAnd while they point to the south as the most likely theatre for a spring counteroffensive, they caution that Ukraine\u2019s effective employment of misinformation and deception in last year\u2019s fighting make feints and so-called demonstrations likely. Among those who have been considering what the new armour arriving in Ukraine may mean for the war is Hamish de Bretton-Gordon, who served as a tank commander with 14th/20th King\u2019s Hussars in the first Gulf war before going on to command the UK\u2019s Joint Chemical, Biological, Radiological and Nuclear Regiment. For De Bretton-Gordon, one of the most significant aspects of the accumulating western armour in Ukraine is the psychological aspect. \u201cPsychologically, it is hugely positive for the Ukrainians and negative for the Russians. If we are just talking about numbers, then on the face of it 50 to 60 modern western tanks in Ukraine is not necessarily a gamechanger. \u201cI\u2019ve said in the past that you would need a minimum of 100 for a big punch, and Ukraine has been talking about needing 300.\u201d\nDespite that, De Bretton-Gordon believes the superior capabilities the tanks offer in comparison with most Russian main battle tanks that have been deployed in Ukraine, and the more effective Ukrainian tactics, will make a difference. \u201cLet\u2019s not forget that these tanks are in addition to the [Soviet and post-Soviet era] T72 tanks Ukraine already has, and they are arriving for a military that has been well trained in manoeuvre warfare and at a time when it is very clear that the Russians are incapable of anything but frontal attacks.\u201d In particular, De Bretton-Gordon says, the ability of Leopards to stand off and fire at greater range, and superior armour in comparison with many of the tanks being fielded by Moscow, may give them an edge. They also have a better ability to operate cross-country in comparison with the lower-clearance Russian tanks and fight effectively at night.\nWith the relatively small numbers available, the question is to what use they will be put. \u201cArmour works best in a shock action breaking [enemy lines],\u201d says De Bretton-Gordon. \u201cThey will want to concentrate firepower rather than disperse it. \u201cThat\u2019s where I think western armour comes in with the rest of the Ukrainian armour following. What feels most logical is something more limited, like breaking the connection between Russian-occupied parts of the Donbas and south. \u201cI can see them wanting to create a widening gap and dislocating the Russians by driving a couple hundred-mile route through that area [south and east of Zaporizhzhia].\u201d Stuart Crawford, who served as an officer in the 4th Royal Tank Regiment, also believes while there are not enough tanks and other western armoured vehicles arriving in Ukraine to make a dramatic difference, they may be useful in a more limited counteroffensive, again pointing towards the south. \u201cIt can\u2019t be the classic punch-a-hole followed by an expanding torrent approach. It\u2019s going to be more localised. And if you are looking at it from a strategic point of view the clear thing that has been talked about is an offensive south from Zaporizhzhia towards Melitopol.\u201d He adds: \u201cThe quality of the tanks arriving is superior to most of the stuff of Russian and Soviet/post-Soviet origin currently being used on both sides.\u201d Crawford is also sceptical of suggestions that having so many different weapons systems coming from different sources will necessarily cause maintenance and logistical problems. \u201cIf you look at the experience of the allies in Normandy in 1944 they had a vast array of equipment from different sources, and still managed. \u201cBut logistically, it probably makes sense to put all the Leopards into the same organisation.\u201d Where Crawford does see a gap, however, is in the potential lack of air support. Neither side has achieved overall air superiority, which is why the Ukrainians have been so vocal in asking for US F16 jets, which are widely available, because without air superiority ground forces cannot move easily. As well as the tanks, Crawford sees the arrival of armoured infantry carriers such as the US Strykers as also being highly useful on a \u201cbattlefield so dominated by artillery\u201d. What does seem clear from Ukrainian statements, and Russian defensive preparations, is that whatever Ukraine is planning to do with its new weapons will be revealed sooner rather than later.", "attributes": {}, "published": true, "createdDate": "2023-04-04T04:00:36Z", "firstPublishedDate": "2023-04-04T04:00:36Z", "publishedDate": "2023-04-04T04:00:36Z", "lastModifiedDate": "2023-04-03T14:54:24Z", "contributors": [], "elements": [{"type": "text", "assets": [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p>In an anonymous muddy field somewhere in Ukraine, the country\u2019s defence minister crouched in the commander\u2019s hatch of a British Challenger 2 tank, one of the first three of 14 to be delivered to Kyiv.</p> \n<p>In a maroon jacket and matching baseball cap, Oleksii Reznikov gave a thumbs-up as he thanked the UK for the delivery of the system.</p> \n<p>It is not only <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/27/ukrainian-troops-return-home-after-challenger-2-tank-training-in-uk\">Challenger main battle tanks</a> that are arriving. It\u2019s going to be more localised. Neither side has achieved overall air superiority, which is why the Ukrainians have been so <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/19/us-f-16s-ukraine-un-ambassador\">vocal in asking for US F16 jets</a>, which are widely available, because without air superiority ground forces cannot move easily.</p> \n<p>As well as the tanks, Crawford sees the arrival of armoured infantry carriers such as the US Strykers as also being highly useful on a \u201cbattlefield so dominated by artillery\u201d.</p> \n<p>What does seem clear from Ukrainian statements, and Russian defensive preparations, a conservative estimate that will only increase without system reforms, according to a government-commissioned independent review.</p> <p>The review into Medicare compliance, led by health economist Dr Pradeep Philip, was commissioned following ongoing concerns about waste and wrongful billing practices.</p> <p>The Philip review found that \u201con a conservative definition of non-compliance and fraud it is entirely feasible the value of non-compliance could exist in the range of $1.5bn to $3bn\u201d.</p> <p>The review makes 23 recommendations, including strengthening the governance model that oversees integrity; removing the veto power of professional bodies in the selection process of the director of the professional services review; ensuring that the legislation that underpins Medicare integrity is amended to be effective and fit for purpose; and improving the detection and disruption of fraud and non-compliance, and mechanisms to address inappropriate and incorrect claiming.</p> <p>In March 2022, Guardian Australia revealed <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/mar/06/medicare-by-providers-is-rampant-and-government-bulk-billing-figures-are-a-lie-review-finds\">illegal billing by doctors and other health providers</a> was inflating bulk-billing rates, findings which were sent to the auditor-general\u2019s office and the then health minister, Greg Hunt.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>That review, authored by Dr Margaret Faux, found a primary cause of Medicare fraud was ignorance by healthcare providers, who did not comprehend the complex billing system and often had no idea they were not complying with regulations. In some cases, the review found, doctors were deliberately doing the wrong thing.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/mar/06/medicare-by-providers-is-rampant-and-government-bulk-billing-figures-are-a-lie-review-finds\">Medicare fraud by providers is rampant and government bulk-billing figures are a \u2018lie\u2019, review finds</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Guardian Australia then sent the Faux review and its key findings to the health minister, Mark Butler, after the federal election. Butler <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/aug/10/not-honest-new-health-minister-dismisses-coalition-election-claim-that-bulk-billing-had-hit-88\">told the Guardian</a> in August the former government had not been honest about the state of bulk billing.</p> <p>Faux\u2019s findings were echoed in the government\u2019s independent review, published on Tuesday. </p> <p>Philip congratulated Faux on her work, saying: \u201cHer work has, on one level, been a great service to the system \u2013 in shining a light on the key issue of trust in our health system.\u201d</p> <p>Philip found \u201cno evidence\u201d of up to $8bn of waste reported by other media who used Faux\u2019s findings to make the claim.</p> <p>But he said the figure he reached \u201ccomes with a significant caveat, in that there is real potential for the problem to scale to the order of magnitude in Dr Faux\u2019s analysis should effective controls, systems and education not be put in place\u201d.</p> <p>Butler said on Tuesday that the problems with Medicare \u201chave been a long time in the making\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cI had been drawing attention before the election to the lack of what I thought was clarity and transparency around bulk-billing data in particular, and we\u2019ve done a lot of work over the last few months to get much clearer data on what is happening,\u201d he said.</p> <p>Butler said he was prepared for some pushback from doctors\u2019 groups about the findings, which he said should not distract from the majority of health professionals who do the right thing and provide a high level of care.</p> <p>\u201cI\u2019m sure there will be some different views and potentially disagreement about the specific recommendations we\u2019ve received from this review,\u201d he said. \u201cBut it\u2019s obviously really important to make sure that every single taxpayer dollar going into the health system is spent well and spent appropriately.\u201d</p> <p><br><br></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/aug/12/bulk-billing-why-is-my-doctor-no-longer-bulk-billed-gp-near-me-medical-centre-is-medicare-in-crisis\">Why is my doctor no longer bulk billing and is Medicare in crisis?</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Philip wrote in his report that the dollar figure on the waste \u201cshould not be the main subject of debate, attractive as that may seem, as the main lesson to learn from this Review is that we must focus on the structural issues and controls in the system, to build trust in Medicare and materially reduce non-compliance and fraud\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cTo this end, some of Dr Faux\u2019s key arguments are important to take seriously as they point to vulnerabilities in the payment system,\u201d the report said.</p> <p>The review also said the billing system must be improved so it is simpler and clearer for health providers to use, recommending better education on Medicare rules, which Faux called for in her review.</p> The review into Medicare compliance, led by health economist Dr Pradeep Philip, was commissioned following ongoing concerns about waste and wrongful billing practices. The Philip review found that \u201con a conservative definition of non-compliance and fraud it is entirely feasible the value of non-compliance could exist in the range of $1.5bn to $3bn\u201d. The review makes 23 recommendations, including strengthening the governance model that oversees integrity; removing the veto power of professional bodies in the selection process of the director of the professional services review; ensuring that the legislation that underpins Medicare integrity is amended to be effective and fit for purpose; and improving the detection and disruption of fraud and non-compliance, and mechanisms to address inappropriate and incorrect claiming. In March 2022, Guardian Australia revealed illegal billing by doctors and other health providers was inflating bulk-billing rates, findings which were sent to the auditor-general\u2019s office and the then health minister, Greg Hunt. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup That review, authored by Dr Margaret Faux, found a primary cause of Medicare fraud was ignorance by healthcare providers, who did not comprehend the complex billing system and often had no idea they were not complying with regulations. In some cases, the review found, doctors were deliberately doing the wrong thing. Guardian Australia then sent the Faux review and its key findings to the health minister, Mark Butler, after the federal election. Butler told the Guardian in August the former government had not been honest about the state of bulk billing. Faux\u2019s findings were echoed in the government\u2019s independent review, published on Tuesday. Philip congratulated Faux on her work, saying: \u201cHer work has, on one level, been a great service to the system \u2013 in shining a light on the key issue of trust in our health system.\u201d Philip found \u201cno evidence\u201d of up to $8bn of waste reported by other media who used Faux\u2019s findings to make the claim. But he said the figure he reached \u201ccomes with a significant caveat, in that there is real potential for the problem to scale to the order of magnitude in Dr Faux\u2019s analysis should effective controls, systems and education not be put in place\u201d. Butler said on Tuesday that the problems with Medicare \u201chave been a long time in the making\u201d. \u201cI had been drawing attention before the election to the lack of what I thought was clarity and transparency around bulk-billing data in particular, and we\u2019ve done a lot of work over the last few months to get much clearer data on what is happening,\u201d he said. Butler said he was prepared for some pushback from doctors\u2019 groups about the findings, which he said should not distract from the majority of health professionals who do the right thing and provide a high level of care. \u201cI\u2019m sure there will be some different views and potentially disagreement about the specific recommendations we\u2019ve received from this review,\u201d he said. \u201cBut it\u2019s obviously really important to make sure that every single taxpayer dollar going into the health system is spent well and spent appropriately.\u201d Philip wrote in his report that the dollar figure on the waste \u201cshould not be the main subject of debate, attractive as that may seem, as the main lesson to learn from this Review is that we must focus on the structural issues and controls in the system, to build trust in Medicare and materially reduce non-compliance and fraud\u201d. \u201cTo this end, some of Dr Faux\u2019s key arguments are important to take seriously as they point to vulnerabilities in the payment system,\u201d the report said. The review also said the billing system must be improved so it is simpler and clearer for health providers to use, recommending better education on Medicare rules, which Faux called for in her review. Butler said the government is now considering all of the recommendations and that some will be more complex to tackle than others. \u201cWe\u2019ll do this properly,\u201d he said. \u201cThat\u2019s obviously not going to be done quickly but we take the recommendations very seriously. "<p>Medicare fraud and non-compliance by doctors is costing taxpayers between $1.5bn and $3bn a year, a conservative estimate that will only increase without system reforms, according to a government-commissioned independent review.</p> <p>The review into Medicare compliance, led by health economist Dr Pradeep Philip, was commissioned following ongoing concerns about waste and wrongful billing practices.</p> <p>The Philip review found that \u201con a conservative definition of non-compliance and fraud it is entirely feasible the value of non-compliance could exist in the range of $1.5bn to $3bn\u201d.</p> <p>The review makes 23 recommendations, including strengthening the governance model that oversees integrity; removing the veto power of professional bodies in the selection process of the director of the professional services review; ensuring that the legislation that underpins Medicare integrity is amended to be effective and fit for purpose; and improving the detection and disruption of fraud and non-compliance, and mechanisms to address inappropriate and incorrect claiming.</p> <p>In March 2022, Guardian Australia revealed <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/mar/06/medicare-by-providers-is-rampant-and-government-bulk-billing-figures-are-a-lie-review-finds\">illegal billing by doctors and other health providers</a> was inflating bulk-billing rates, findings which were sent to the auditor-general\u2019s office and the then health minister, Greg Hunt.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>That review, authored by Dr Margaret Faux, found a primary cause of Medicare fraud was ignorance by healthcare providers, who did not comprehend the complex billing system and often had no idea they were not complying with regulations. In some cases, the review found, doctors were deliberately doing the wrong thing.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/mar/06/medicare-by-providers-is-rampant-and-government-bulk-billing-figures-are-a-lie-review-finds\">Medicare fraud by providers is rampant and government bulk-billing figures are a \u2018lie\u2019, review finds</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Guardian Australia then sent the Faux review and its key findings to the health minister, Mark Butler, after the federal election. Butler <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/aug/10/not-honest-new-health-minister-dismisses-coalition-election-claim-that-bulk-billing-had-hit-88\">told the Guardian</a> in August the former government had not been honest about the state of bulk billing.</p> <p>Faux\u2019s findings were echoed in the government\u2019s independent review, published on Tuesday. </p> <p>Philip congratulated Faux on her work, saying: \u201cHer work has, on one level, been a great service to the system \u2013 in shining a light on the key issue of trust in our health system.\u201d</p> <p>Philip found \u201cno evidence\u201d of up to $8bn of waste reported by other media who used Faux\u2019s findings to make the claim.</p> <p>But he said the figure he reached \u201ccomes with a significant caveat, in that there is real potential for the problem to scale to the order of magnitude in Dr Faux\u2019s analysis should effective controls, systems and education not be put in place\u201d.</p> <p>Butler said on Tuesday that the problems with Medicare \u201chave been a long time in the making\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cI had been drawing attention before the election to the lack of what I thought was clarity and transparency around bulk-billing data in particular, and we\u2019ve done a lot of work over the last few months to get much clearer data on what is happening,\u201d he said.</p> <p>Butler said he was prepared for some pushback from doctors\u2019 groups about the findings, which he said should not distract from the majority of health professionals who do the right thing and provide a high level of care.</p> <p>\u201cI\u2019m sure there will be some different views and potentially disagreement about the specific recommendations we\u2019ve received from this review,\u201d he said. \u201cBut it\u2019s obviously really important to make sure that every single taxpayer dollar going into the health system is spent well and spent appropriately.\u201d</p> <p><br><br></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/aug/12/bulk-billing-why-is-my-doctor-no-longer-bulk-billed-gp-near-me-medical-centre-is-medicare-in-crisis\">Why is my doctor no longer bulk billing and is Medicare in crisis?</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Philip wrote in his report that the dollar figure on the waste \u201cshould not be the main subject of debate, attractive as that may seem, as the main lesson to learn from this Review is that we must focus on the structural issues and controls in the system, to build trust in Medicare and materially reduce non-compliance and fraud\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cTo this end, some of Dr Faux\u2019s key arguments are important to take seriously as they point to vulnerabilities in the payment system,\u201d the report said.</p> <p>The review also said the billing system must be improved so it is simpler and clearer for health providers to use, recommending better education on Medicare rules, which Faux called for in her review.</p> <p>Butler said the government is now considering all of the recommendations and that some will be more complex to tackle than others.</p> <p>\u201cWe\u2019ll do this properly,\u201d he said. \u201cThat\u2019s obviously not going to be done quickly but we take the recommendations very seriously. Some recommendations are straightforward, but overall this is an extraordinarily complex era of public policy.\u201d<br></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Medicare fraud and non-compliance by doctors is costing taxpayers between $1.5bn and $3bn a year, a conservative estimate that will only increase without system reforms, according to a government-commissioned independent review. The review into Medicare compliance, led by health economist Dr Pradeep Philip, was commissioned following ongoing concerns about waste and wrongful billing practices. The Philip review found that \u201con a conservative definition of non-compliance and fraud it is entirely feasible the value of non-compliance could exist in the range of $1.5bn to $3bn\u201d. The review makes 23 recommendations, including strengthening the governance model that oversees integrity; removing the veto power of professional bodies in the selection process of the director of the professional services review; ensuring that the legislation that underpins Medicare integrity is amended to be effective and fit for purpose; and improving the detection and disruption of fraud and non-compliance, and mechanisms to address inappropriate and incorrect claiming. In March 2022, Guardian Australia revealed illegal billing by doctors and other health providers was inflating bulk-billing rates, findings which were sent to the auditor-general\u2019s office and the then health minister, Greg Hunt. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup That review, authored by Dr Margaret Faux, found a primary cause of Medicare fraud was ignorance by healthcare providers, who did not comprehend the complex billing system and often had no idea they were not complying with regulations. In some cases, the review found, doctors were deliberately doing the wrong thing.\nGuardian Australia then sent the Faux review and its key findings to the health minister, Mark Butler, after the federal election. Butler told the Guardian in August the former government had not been honest about the state of bulk billing. Faux\u2019s findings were echoed in the government\u2019s independent review, published on Tuesday. Philip congratulated Faux on her work, saying: \u201cHer work has, on one level, been a great service to the system \u2013 in shining a light on the key issue of trust in our health system.\u201d Philip found \u201cno evidence\u201d of up to $8bn of waste reported by other media who used Faux\u2019s findings to make the claim. But he said the figure he reached \u201ccomes with a significant caveat, in that there is real potential for the problem to scale to the order of magnitude in Dr Faux\u2019s analysis should effective controls, systems and education not be put in place\u201d. Butler said on Tuesday that the problems with Medicare \u201chave been a long time in the making\u201d. \u201cI had been drawing attention before the election to the lack of what I thought was clarity and transparency around bulk-billing data in particular, and we\u2019ve done a lot of work over the last few months to get much clearer data on what is happening,\u201d he said. Butler said he was prepared for some pushback from doctors\u2019 groups about the findings, which he said should not distract from the majority of health professionals who do the right thing and provide a high level of care. \u201cI\u2019m sure there will be some different views and potentially disagreement about the specific recommendations we\u2019ve received from this review,\u201d he said. \u201cBut it\u2019s obviously really important to make sure that every single taxpayer dollar going into the health system is spent well and spent appropriately.\u201d\nPhilip wrote in his report that the dollar figure on the waste \u201cshould not be the main subject of debate, attractive as that may seem, as the main lesson to learn from this Review is that we must focus on the structural issues and controls in the system, to build trust in Medicare and materially reduce non-compliance and fraud\u201d. \u201cTo this end, some of Dr Faux\u2019s key arguments are important to take seriously as they point to vulnerabilities in the payment system,\u201d the report said. The review also said the billing system must be improved so it is simpler and clearer for health providers to use, recommending better education on Medicare rules, which Faux called for in her review. Butler said the government is now considering all of the recommendations and that some will be more complex to tackle than others. \u201cWe\u2019ll do this properly,\u201d he said. \u201cThat\u2019s obviously not going to be done quickly but we take the recommendations very seriously. While not every nurse who registers will make the move, they make up about 8% of the approximately 65,000 nurses registered in New Zealand.</p> <p>\u201cTo see that volume of numbers of nurses interested in registering in Australia \u2013 it was an absolute shock,\u201d said Kerri Nuku, a <em>kaiwhakahaere</em> [senior organiser] at nursing union the New Zealand Nurses Organisation.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/sep/15/queensland-greens-push-for-free-nursing-and-midwifery-degrees-to-combat-staff-shortages\">Queensland Greens push for free nursing and midwifery degrees to combat staff shortages</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The impact of losing large numbers of nurses would be \u201chuge \u2013 not only in size, but also in skill and experience\u201d, she said, compounding existing shortages and a workforce experiencing high rates of burnout and attrition. Vacancies in New Zealand\u2019s healthcare workforce were at 11% last year \u2013 up from a typical rate of about 5% to 6%.</p> <p>As more nurses left, Nuku said, it could create a vicious cycle of overwork, with already stretched health workers forced to take on longer shifts and larger workloads.</p> <p>\u201cNurses have absolutely been feeling the pressure and workforce shortages,\u201d Nuku said, particularly after the pressures of the Covid-19 pandemic response, and responses to natural disasters like Cyclone Gabrielle.</p> <p>She said that while pay rates varied across New Zealand and Australia, she had spoken to some nurses who had moved to Australia and were now earning almost triple New Zealand rates, particularly over holiday seasons and in smaller mining towns.</p> <p>\u201cWe\u2019ve already got new vacancies across the country,\u201d Nuku said. \u201cWe want them to be filled by experienced nurses \u2013 so if we\u2019re losing or leaking our own workforce, as they look overseas, if they\u2019re constantly [overworked] or tired \u2013 the quality of care that we\u2019re able to provide to patients will not be a suitable standard.\u201d</p> <p>Opposition spokesperson Dr Shane Reti said he was not surprised to see local nurses looking at moving offshore, saying pay rates in Australia were broadly higher and the country was in the midst of a recruitment drive, with states pledging billions to build a larger health workforce.</p> <p>\u201cThe health system is so broken in New Zealand at the moment,\u201d he said. \u201cI do understand why New Zealand nurses may want to go offshore to not have to confront what we\u2019re seeing now already before winter starts.\u201d</p> <p>But Reti would not say whether higher pay would be required to solve New Zealand\u2019s healthcare shortages.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a recruits overseas health workers, their home nations bear the cost</a> </p> </aside>  <p>New Zealand nurses held a series of strike actions in late 2022, and are planning a national day of action in mid-April, with coordinated national protests to call for more nurses and better pay. Last week, New Zealand\u2019s government announced a pay boost of up to 15% for community-based nurses, to try to compete with the Australian sector, where <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/04/australias-aged-care-workers-win-15-pay-rise-and-minister-says-its-the-first-step\">aged care nurses won a 15% pay rise</a> in November 2022.</p> <p>The government said in a statement that the increases \u201cmade wages competitive with Australian nurse wages\u201d, and the associate health minister, Dr Ayesha Verrall, said it \u201cwill ensure workers are paid fairly for their critical roles in protecting the health and wellbeing of all New Zealanders\u201d.</p> <p>The government has also been carrying out health sector reform to merge district health boards into a single national agency, which the health minister, Andrew Little, said last year \u201cwill be better able to plan for and manage the nursing workforce right across the country\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIt is critically important that we fix the nursing shortage,\u201d Little said at the time.</p> <p>The chief people officer at Te Whatu Ora/Health New Zealand, Andrew Slater, said the number from Ahpra was \u201ca concern if it resulted in people taking up jobs overseas\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cWe need every nurse we can get in Aotearoa,\u201d he Almost 5,000 New Zealand nurses have registered to practise in Australia since August, a spokesperson from the Australian Health Practitioner Regulation Agency (Ahpra) and the Nursing and Midwifery Board of Australia confirmed on Tuesday. While not every nurse who registers will make the move, they make up about 8% of the approximately 65,000 nurses registered in New Zealand. \u201cTo see that volume of numbers of nurses interested in registering in Australia \u2013 it was an absolute shock,\u201d said Kerri Nuku, a kaiwhakahaere [senior organiser] at nursing union the New Zealand Nurses Organisation. While not every nurse who registers will make the move, they make up about 8% of the approximately 65,000 nurses registered in New Zealand.</p> <p>\u201cTo see that volume of numbers of nurses interested in registering in Australia \u2013 it was an absolute shock,\u201d said Kerri Nuku, a <em>kaiwhakahaere</em> [senior organiser] at nursing union the New Zealand Nurses Organisation.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/sep/15/queensland-greens-push-for-free-nursing-and-midwifery-degrees-to-combat-staff-shortages\">Queensland Greens push for free nursing and midwifery degrees to combat staff shortages</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The impact of losing large numbers of nurses would be \u201chuge \u2013 not only in size, but also in skill and experience\u201d, she said, compounding existing shortages and a workforce experiencing high rates of burnout and attrition. Vacancies in New Zealand\u2019s healthcare workforce were at 11% last year \u2013 up from a typical rate of about 5% to 6%.</p> <p>As more nurses left, Nuku said, it could create a vicious cycle of overwork, with already stretched health workers forced to take on longer shifts and larger workloads.</p> <p>\u201cNurses have absolutely been feeling the pressure and workforce shortages,\u201d Nuku said, particularly after the pressures of the Covid-19 pandemic response, and responses to natural disasters like Cyclone Gabrielle.</p> <p>She said that while pay rates varied across New Zealand and Australia, she had spoken to some nurses who had moved to Australia and were now earning almost triple New Zealand rates, particularly over holiday seasons and in smaller mining towns.</p> <p>\u201cWe\u2019ve already got new vacancies across the country,\u201d Nuku said. \u201cWe want them to be filled by experienced nurses \u2013 so if we\u2019re losing or leaking our own workforce, as they look overseas, if they\u2019re constantly [overworked] or tired \u2013 the quality of care that we\u2019re able to provide to patients will not be a suitable standard.\u201d</p> <p>Opposition spokesperson Dr Shane Reti said he was not surprised to see local nurses looking at moving offshore, saying pay rates in Australia were broadly higher and the country was in the midst of a recruitment drive, with states pledging billions to build a larger health workforce.</p> <p>\u201cThe health system is so broken in New Zealand at the moment,\u201d he said. \u201cI do understand why New Zealand nurses may want to go offshore to not have to confront what we\u2019re seeing now already before winter starts.\u201d</p> <p>But Reti would not say whether higher pay would be required to solve New Zealand\u2019s healthcare shortages.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2022/jun/26/a-finite-resource-as-australia-recruits-overseas-health-workers-their-home-nations-bear-the-cost\">\u2018A finite resource\u2019: as Australia recruits overseas health workers, their home nations bear the cost</a> </p> </aside>  <p>New Zealand nurses held a series of strike actions in late 2022, and are planning a national day of action in mid-April, with coordinated national protests to call for more nurses and better pay. Last week, New Zealand\u2019s government announced a pay boost of up to 15% for community-based nurses, to try to compete with the Australian sector, where <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/04/australias-aged-care-workers-win-15-pay-rise-and-minister-says-its-the-first-step\">aged care nurses won a 15% pay rise</a> in November 2022.</p> <p>The government said in a statement that the increases \u201cmade wages competitive with Australian nurse wages\u201d, and the associate health minister, Dr Ayesha Verrall, said it \u201cwill ensure workers are paid fairly for their critical roles in protecting the health and wellbeing of all New Zealanders\u201d.</p> <p>The government has also been carrying out health sector reform to merge district health boards into a single national agency, which the health minister, Andrew Little, said last year \u201cwill be better able to plan for and manage the nursing workforce right across the country\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIt is critically important that we fix the nursing shortage,\u201d Little said at the time.</p> <p>The chief people officer at Te Whatu Ora/Health New Zealand, Andrew Slater, said the number from Ahpra was \u201ca concern if it resulted in people taking up jobs overseas\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cWe need every nurse we can get in Aotearoa,\u201d he said.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Thousands of New Zealand nurses are registering to work in Australia in pursuit of better pay and conditions, amid staffing shortages and industrial action in their home country. Almost 5,000 New Zealand nurses have registered to practise in Australia since August, a spokesperson from the Australian Health Practitioner Regulation Agency (Ahpra) and the Nursing and Midwifery Board of Australia confirmed on Tuesday. While not every nurse who registers will make the move, they make up about 8% of the approximately 65,000 nurses registered in New Zealand. \u201cTo see that volume of numbers of nurses interested in registering in Australia \u2013 it was an absolute shock,\u201d said Kerri Nuku, a kaiwhakahaere [senior organiser] at nursing union the New Zealand Nurses Organisation.\nThe impact of losing large numbers of nurses would be \u201chuge \u2013 not only in size, but also in skill and experience\u201d, she said, compounding existing shortages and a workforce experiencing high rates of burnout and attrition. Vacancies in New Zealand\u2019s healthcare workforce were at 11% last year \u2013 up from a typical rate of about 5% to 6%. As more nurses left, Nuku said, it could create a vicious cycle of overwork, with already stretched health workers forced to take on longer shifts and larger workloads. \u201cNurses have absolutely been feeling the pressure and workforce shortages,\u201d Nuku said, particularly after the pressures of the Covid-19 pandemic response, and responses to natural disasters like Cyclone Gabrielle. She said that while pay rates varied across New Zealand and Australia, she had spoken to some nurses who had moved to Australia and were now earning almost triple New Zealand rates, particularly over holiday seasons and in smaller mining towns. \u201cWe\u2019ve already got new vacancies across the country,\u201d Nuku said. \u201cWe want them to be filled by experienced nurses \u2013 so if we\u2019re losing or leaking our own workforce, as they look overseas, if they\u2019re constantly [overworked] or tired \u2013 the quality of care that we\u2019re able to provide to patients will not be a suitable standard.\u201d Opposition spokesperson Dr Shane Reti said he was not surprised to see local nurses looking at moving offshore, saying pay rates in Australia were broadly higher and the country was in the midst of a recruitment drive, with states pledging billions to build a larger health workforce. \u201cThe health system is so broken in New Zealand at the moment,\u201d he said. \u201cI do understand why New Zealand nurses may want to go offshore to not have to confront what we\u2019re seeing now already before winter starts.\u201d But Reti would not say whether higher pay would be required to solve New Zealand\u2019s healthcare shortages.\nNew Zealand nurses held a series of strike actions in late 2022, and are planning a national day of action in mid-April, with coordinated national protests to call for more nurses and better pay. Last week, New Zealand\u2019s government announced a pay boost of up to 15% for community-based nurses, to try to compete with the Australian sector, where aged care nurses won a 15% pay rise in November 2022. But can it escape the symbolism of being on stage next to Pauline Hanson?", "byline": "Gabrielle Chan", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"44e4044f51041b9302fac7857b0d1a696049c1a3\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/44e4044f51041b9302fac7857b0d1a696049c1a3/1025_829_2209_1325/1000.jpg\" alt=\"(L-R) Barnaby Joyce, former
