class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Tom Plevey</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>Just four years ago, Tim Fischer called on the National party to <a href=\"https://www.abc.net.au/news/2019-03-27/tim-fischer-says-one-nation-should-pay-preference-price/10946118\">put One Nation last</a> on its how-to-vote cards.</p> <p>The late Nationals elder and former deputy prime minister had long held the line against One Nation\u2019s divisive rhetoric from Pauline Hanson\u2019s first entry into parliament in 1996.</p> <p>Fischer was joined by fellow Nationals members such as former Queensland senator Ron Boswell, who in his <a href=\"https://australianpolitics.com/2014/06/17/valedictory-speech-senator-ron-boswell.html\">farewell speech</a> told parliament that his efforts to defeat Hanson and her party were his \u201cgreatest achievement\u201d.</p> <p>Current Nationals have had less clarity on One Nation, in spite of the party\u2019s history of singling out groups for their heritage or religion, whether it be <a href=\"https://www.abc.net.au/news/2016-07-10/timeline-rise-of-pauline-hanson-one-nation/7583230\">Indigenous Australians</a>, <a href=\"https://www.abc.net.au/news/2016-09-14/pauline-hanson-maiden-speech-asian-immigration/7645578\">Asian Australians</a> or <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2017/aug/17/pauline-hanson-wears-burqa-in-australian-senate-while-calling-for-ban\">Muslim Australians</a>.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/commentisfree/2023/mar/09/the-future-of-my-grandchildren-is-in-the-hands-of-all-australians-help-bring-our-dream-alive\">The future of my grandchildren is in the hands of all Australians. Help bring our dream alive | Roy Gibson</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Barnaby Joyce told his constituents in the 2022 election that One Nation had changed,<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/may/10/barnaby-joyce-one-nation-preferences-pauline-hanson-how-to-vote-card-australia-election-2022\"> that the party of 10 or 15 years ago was \u201cnot the party of today\u201d</a>, as he justified his decision to preference the One Nation candidate.</p> <p>But it was a step closer when Joyce joined Hanson, broadcaster Alan Jones and former Labor minister Gary Johns for an event on Friday in his New England electorate, advertised as helping people to \u201cunderstand why you should oppose the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/24/what-is-the-indigenous-voice-to-parliament-wording-referendum-question-constitution-change-details-australia-vote-how-would-it-work-what-does-it-mean-explainer\">voice to parliament</a>\u201d.</p> <p>The reality was it placed Joyce on stage with someone his own Indigenous constituents accused of using <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/no-campaign-against-indigenous-voice-criticised-for-using-really-dehumanising-language\">racist talking points and \u201creally dehumanising\u201d language</a>.</p> <p>Held in Tamworth, it was the first regional event of the Recognise A Better Way group, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/feb/20/voice-referendum-whos-behind-the-yes-and-no-campaigns-and-how-do-they-plan-to-convince-australia\">one of the most high-profile organisations campaigning against the voice.</a></p> <p>The group is led by Nyunggai Warren Mundine and its committee has Indigenous and non-Indigenous members, although none of the Indigenous committee members spoke in Tamworth that night.</p> <p>Whatever you think of the protagonists or the Indigenous voice to parliament, the speaker list promised controversy. In doing so, it provided an audience to hang a lantern over the no campaign.</p> <p>The purpose of that spotlight in a rural community is to show those wavering that there may be a critical mass against the voice, who may be people they know \u2013 people who have influence within their own social circles.</p> <p>So with First Nations people in the audience rather than on stage, regional Australia got a taste of what might lie ahead as we debate the proposed Indigenous voice.</p> <p>The political strategy boiled down to this slogan: if you don\u2019t know, vote no \u2013 reminiscent of the republic referendum and designed to sow doubt. But the idea was bedded down with <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/no-campaign-against-indigenous-voice-criticised-for-using-really-dehumanising-language\">spurious claims from the speakers and long forgotten terms like \u201cintermarriage\u201d</a>.</p> <p>It is likely these people will lead the no campaign in regional areas with Joyce as a key protagonist. Indeed Joyce told the audience, which was <a href=\"https://www.northerndailyleader.com.au/story/8143642/voices-heard-at-launch-of-no-vote-for-voice-to-parliament/\">a mix of constituents and visitors from as far as Western Australia</a>, that he was \u201cpassionate\u201d in his opposition.</p> <p>It is four months since the Nationals leader, David Littleproud, fronted the cameras with Country Liberal party senator Jacinta Nampijinpa Price and most of his party to reveal the decision to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/28/national-party-wont-support-indigenous-voice-to-parliament-saying-it-wont-close-the-gap\">oppose the voice</a>, claiming it would not close the gap for Indigenous people.</p> <p>The Calare MP <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/23/nationals-mp-andrew-gee-quits-party-citing-opposition-to-indigenous-voice#:~:text=Nationals%20MP%20Andrew%20Gee%20quits%20party%20citing%20its%20opposition%20to%20Indigenous%20voice,-This%20article%20is&amp;text=The%20Nationals%20MP%20Andrew%20Gee,the%20handling%20of%20natural%20disasters.\">Andrew Gee quit the Nationals</a> days later to support the voice and sit as an independent in a seat with a rising tree-change demographic.</p> <p>Since then, the Nationals as a party have avoided front-running the no case. But in contrast with Littleproud, Joyce is keen to take part and is arguing on different grounds from closing the gap.</p> <p>\u201cIf one group has a right based on their DNA, another group does not have that right,\u201d Joyce told the Tamworth crowd.</p> <p>Price is the other key voice of conservative MPs, as the face of the Fair Australia campaign against the voice. She has argued her consultations with people in remote areas show they either didn\u2019t know about the voice or didn\u2019t want it.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>It is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas.</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>This will leave Joyce and Price as the two rural MPs most associated with the no case.</p> <p>And what is clear in regional areas is the desire for detail flagged by audiences that both support and oppose the voice \u2013 a fact that Joyce is using. He described the proposal as \u201csneaky\u201d for lacking detail, even if he then painted a very detailed and dire picture of what the voice may become.</p> <p>Just weeks ago, the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/16/they-want-to-know-how-things-work-pat-dodson-buoyed-up-by-first-regional-victorian-voice-forum\">first regional forum</a> was held with Labor senator and special envoy for reconciliation Pat Dodson in Ballarat. The audience appeared to be in favour but they were also hungry for the practicalities.</p> <p>\u201cPeople who live in the regions are far more practical,\u201d he told <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/16/they-want-to-know-how-things-work-pat-dodson-buoyed-up-by-first-regional-victorian-voice-forum\">Guardian Australia</a>.</p> <p>\u201cThey want to know how things work. Whereas we\u2019re talking about a principle here, which is significant because it\u2019s about the constitution. In a moral sense and an ethical sense, is this a good thing for us to do?\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/03/yunupingu-yolu-leader-and-campaigner-for-indigenous-rights-dies-aged-74\">Yunupingu, Yol\u014bu leader and campaigner for Indigenous rights, dies aged 74</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Other campaigns are rolling out, including one from the folks who <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/04/indigenous-voice-to-parliament-regional-australia-national-party\">bought you the Voices For movement</a> \u2013 with a series of kitchen table conversations. Under the banner <a href=\"https://togetheryes.com.au/\">Together, Yes</a>, these groups are designed to promote quieter, respectful conversations inside homes that fly under the radar of the noisy media debate.</p> <p>The voice process has been a long time in the making. The list of community groups consulted during the <a href=\"https://voice.niaa.gov.au/sites/default/files/2021-12/indigenous-voice-co-design-process-final-report_1.pdf\">co-design phase of the voice</a> is long.</p> <p>Yet for all that consultation, it is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas. While that is sometimes the nature of democracy, the key questions is how voters in 2023 will react to divisive politics and how politicians write their own legacies.</p>", "wordcount": "920", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T02:28:44Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T07:26:47Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nkn58", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/44e4044f51041b9302fac7857b0d1a696049c1a3/1025_829_2209_1325/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "Just four years ago, Tim Fischer called on the National party to put One Nation last on its how-to-vote cards. The late Nationals elder and former deputy prime minister had long held the line against One Nation\u2019s divisive rhetoric from Pauline Hanson\u2019s first entry into parliament in 1996. Fischer was joined by fellow Nationals members such as former Queensland senator Ron Boswell, who in his farewell speech told parliament that his efforts to defeat Hanson and her party were his \u201cgreatest achievement\u201d. Current Nationals have had less clarity on One Nation, in spite of the party\u2019s history of singling out groups for their heritage or religion, whether it be Indigenous Australians, Asian Australians or Muslim Australians. Barnaby Joyce told his constituents in the 2022 election that One Nation had changed, that the party of 10 or 15 years ago was \u201cnot the party of today\u201d, as he justified his decision to preference the One Nation candidate. But it was a step closer when Joyce joined Hanson, broadcaster Alan Jones and former Labor minister Gary Johns for an event on Friday in his New England electorate, advertised as helping people to \u201cunderstand why you should oppose the voice to parliament\u201d. The reality was it placed Joyce on stage with someone his own Indigenous constituents accused of using racist talking points and \u201creally dehumanising\u201d language. Held in Tamworth, it was the first regional event of the Recognise A Better Way group, one of the most high-profile organisations campaigning against the voice. The group is led by Nyunggai Warren Mundine and its committee has Indigenous and non-Indigenous members, although none of the Indigenous committee members spoke in Tamworth that night. Whatever you think of the protagonists or the Indigenous voice to parliament, the speaker list promised controversy. In doing so, it provided an audience to hang a lantern over the no campaign. The purpose of that spotlight in a rural community is to show those wavering that there may be a critical mass against the voice, who may be people they know \u2013 people who have influence within their own social circles. So with First Nations people in the audience rather than on stage, regional Australia got a taste of what might lie ahead as we debate the proposed Indigenous voice. The political strategy boiled down to this slogan: if you don\u2019t know, vote no \u2013 reminiscent of the republic referendum and designed to sow doubt. But the idea was bedded down with spurious claims from the speakers and long forgotten terms like \u201cintermarriage\u201d. It is likely these people will lead the no campaign in regional areas with Joyce as a key protagonist. Indeed Joyce told the audience, which was a mix of constituents and visitors from as far as Western Australia, that he was \u201cpassionate\u201d in his opposition. It is four months since the Nationals leader, David Littleproud, fronted the cameras with Country Liberal party senator Jacinta Nampijinpa Price and most of his party to reveal the decision to oppose the voice, claiming it would not close the gap for Indigenous people. The Calare MP Andrew Gee quit the Nationals days later to support the voice and sit as an independent in a seat with a rising tree-change demographic. Since then, the Nationals as a party have avoided front-running the no case. But in contrast with Littleproud, Joyce is keen to take part and is arguing on different grounds from closing the gap. \u201cIf one group has a right based on their DNA, another group does not have that right,\u201d Joyce told the Tamworth crowd. Price is the other key voice of conservative MPs, as the face of the Fair Australia campaign against the voice. She has argued her consultations with people in remote areas show they either didn\u2019t know about the voice or didn\u2019t want it. This will leave Joyce and Price as the two rural MPs most associated with the no case. And what is clear in regional areas is the desire for detail flagged by audiences that both support and oppose the voice \u2013 a fact that Joyce is using. He described the proposal as \u201csneaky\u201d for lacking detail, even if he then painted a very detailed and dire picture of what the voice may become. Just weeks ago, the first regional forum was held with Labor senator and special envoy for reconciliation Pat Dodson in Ballarat. The audience appeared to be in favour but they were also hungry for the practicalities. \u201cPeople who live in the regions are far more practical,\u201d he told Guardian Australia. \u201cThey want to know how things work. Whereas we\u2019re talking about a principle here, which is significant because it\u2019s about the constitution. In a moral sense and an ethical sense, is this a good thing for us to do?\u201d Other campaigns are rolling out, including one from the folks who bought you the Voices For movement \u2013 with a series of kitchen table conversations. Under the banner Together, Yes, these groups are designed to promote quieter, respectful conversations inside homes that fly under the radar of the noisy media debate. The voice process has been a long time in the making. The list of community groups consulted during the co-design phase of the voice is long. Yet for all that consultation, it is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas. In doing so, it provided an audience to hang a lantern over the no campaign.</p> <p>The purpose of that spotlight in a rural community is to show those wavering that there may be a critical mass against the voice, who may be people they know \u2013 people who have influence within their own social circles.</p> <p>So with First Nations people in the audience rather than on stage, regional Australia got a taste of what might lie ahead as we debate the proposed Indigenous voice.</p> <p>The political strategy boiled down to this slogan: if you don\u2019t know, vote no \u2013 reminiscent of the republic referendum and designed to sow doubt. But the idea was bedded down with <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/no-campaign-against-indigenous-voice-criticised-for-using-really-dehumanising-language\">spurious claims from the speakers and long forgotten terms like \u201cintermarriage\u201d</a>.</p> <p>It is likely these people will lead the no campaign in regional areas with Joyce as a key protagonist. Indeed Joyce told the audience, which was <a href=\"https://www.northerndailyleader.com.au/story/8143642/voices-heard-at-launch-of-no-vote-for-voice-to-parliament/\">a mix of constituents and visitors from as far as Western Australia</a>, that he was \u201cpassionate\u201d in his opposition.</p> <p>It is four months since the Nationals leader, David Littleproud, fronted the cameras with Country Liberal party senator Jacinta Nampijinpa Price and most of his party to reveal the decision to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/28/national-party-wont-support-indigenous-voice-to-parliament-saying-it-wont-close-the-gap\">oppose the voice</a>, claiming it would not close the gap for Indigenous people.</p> <p>The Calare MP <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/23/nationals-mp-andrew-gee-quits-party-citing-opposition-to-indigenous-voice#:~:text=Nationals%20MP%20Andrew%20Gee%20quits%20party%20citing%20its%20opposition%20to%20Indigenous%20voice,-This%20article%20is&amp;text=The%20Nationals%20MP%20Andrew%20Gee,the%20handling%20of%20natural%20disasters.\">Andrew Gee quit the Nationals</a> days later to support the voice and sit as an independent in a seat with a rising tree-change demographic.</p> <p>Since then, the Nationals as a party have avoided front-running the no case. But in contrast with Littleproud, Joyce is keen to take part and is arguing on different grounds from closing the gap.</p> <p>\u201cIf one group has a right based on their DNA, another group does not have that right,\u201d Joyce told the Tamworth crowd.</p> <p>Price is the other key voice of conservative MPs, as the face of the Fair Australia campaign against the voice. She has argued her consultations with people in remote areas show they either didn\u2019t know about the voice or didn\u2019t want it.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>It is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas.</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>This will leave Joyce and Price as the two rural MPs most associated with the no case.</p> <p>And what is clear in regional areas is the desire for detail flagged by audiences that both support and oppose the voice \u2013 a fact that Joyce is using. He described the proposal as \u201csneaky\u201d for lacking detail, even if he then painted a very detailed and dire picture of what the voice may become.</p> <p>Just weeks ago, the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/16/they-want-to-know-how-things-work-pat-dodson-buoyed-up-by-first-regional-victorian-voice-forum\">first regional forum</a> was held with Labor senator and special envoy for reconciliation Pat Dodson in Ballarat. The audience appeared to be in favour but they were also hungry for the practicalities.</p> <p>\u201cPeople who live in the regions are far more practical,\u201d he told <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/16/they-want-to-know-how-things-work-pat-dodson-buoyed-up-by-first-regional-victorian-voice-forum\">Guardian Australia</a>.</p> <p>\u201cThey want to know how things work. Whereas we\u2019re talking about a principle here, which is significant because it\u2019s about the constitution. In a moral sense and an ethical sense, is this a good thing for us to do?\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/03/yunupingu-yolu-leader-and-campaigner-for-indigenous-rights-dies-aged-74\">Yunupingu, Yol\u014bu leader and campaigner for Indigenous rights, dies aged 74</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Other campaigns are rolling out, including one from the folks who <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/04/indigenous-voice-to-parliament-regional-australia-national-party\">bought you the Voices For movement</a> \u2013 with a series of kitchen table conversations. Under the banner <a href=\"https://togetheryes.com.au/\">Together, Yes</a>, these groups are designed to promote quieter, respectful conversations inside homes that fly under the radar of the noisy media debate.</p> <p>The voice process has been a long time in the making. The list of community groups consulted during the <a href=\"https://voice.niaa.gov.au/sites/default/files/2021-12/indigenous-voice-co-design-process-final-report_1.pdf\">co-design phase of the voice</a> is long.</p> <p>Yet for all that consultation, it is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas. While that is sometimes the nature of democracy, the key questions is how voters in 2023 will react to divisive politics and how politicians write their own legacies.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Just four years ago, Tim Fischer called on the National party to put One Nation last on its how-to-vote cards. The late Nationals elder and former deputy prime minister had long held the line against One Nation\u2019s divisive rhetoric from Pauline Hanson\u2019s first entry into parliament in 1996. Fischer was joined by fellow Nationals members such as former Queensland senator Ron Boswell, who in his farewell speech told parliament that his efforts to defeat Hanson and her party were his \u201cgreatest achievement\u201d. Current Nationals have had less clarity on One Nation, in spite of the party\u2019s history of singling out groups for their heritage or religion, whether it be Indigenous Australians, Asian Australians or Muslim Australians.\nBarnaby Joyce told his constituents in the 2022 election that One Nation had changed, that the party of 10 or 15 years ago was \u201cnot the party of today\u201d, as he justified his decision to preference the One Nation candidate. But it was a step closer when Joyce joined Hanson, broadcaster Alan Jones and former Labor minister Gary Johns for an event on Friday in his New England electorate, advertised as helping people to \u201cunderstand why you should oppose the voice to parliament\u201d. The reality was it placed Joyce on stage with someone his own Indigenous constituents accused of using racist talking points and \u201creally dehumanising\u201d language. Held in Tamworth, it was the first regional event of the Recognise A Better Way group, one of the most high-profile organisations campaigning against the voice. The group is led by Nyunggai Warren Mundine and its committee has Indigenous and non-Indigenous members, although none of the Indigenous committee members spoke in Tamworth that night. Whatever you think of the protagonists or the Indigenous voice to parliament, the speaker list promised controversy. In doing so, it provided an audience to hang a lantern over the no campaign. The purpose of that spotlight in a rural community is to show those wavering that there may be a critical mass against the voice, who may be people they know \u2013 people who have influence within their own social circles. So with First Nations people in the audience rather than on stage, regional Australia got a taste of what might lie ahead as we debate the proposed Indigenous voice. The political strategy boiled down to this slogan: if you don\u2019t know, vote no \u2013 reminiscent of the republic referendum and designed to sow doubt. But the idea was bedded down with spurious claims from the speakers and long forgotten terms like \u201cintermarriage\u201d. It is likely these people will lead the no campaign in regional areas with Joyce as a key protagonist. Indeed Joyce told the audience, which was a mix of constituents and visitors from as far as Western Australia, that he was \u201cpassionate\u201d in his opposition. It is four months since the Nationals leader, David Littleproud, fronted the cameras with Country Liberal party senator Jacinta Nampijinpa Price and most of his party to reveal the decision to oppose the voice, claiming it would not close the gap for Indigenous people. The Calare MP Andrew Gee quit the Nationals days later to support the voice and sit as an independent in a seat with a rising tree-change demographic. Since then, the Nationals as a party have avoided front-running the no case. But in contrast with Littleproud, Joyce is keen to take part and is arguing on different grounds from closing the gap. \u201cIf one group has a right based on their DNA, another group does not have that right,\u201d Joyce told the Tamworth crowd. Price is the other key voice of conservative MPs, as the face of the Fair Australia campaign against the voice. She has argued her consultations with people in remote areas show they either didn\u2019t know about the voice or didn\u2019t want it.\nThis will leave Joyce and Price as the two rural MPs most associated with the no case. And what is clear in regional areas is the desire for detail flagged by audiences that both support and oppose the voice \u2013 a fact that Joyce is using. He described the proposal as \u201csneaky\u201d for lacking detail, even if he then painted a very detailed and dire picture of what the voice may become. Just weeks ago, the first regional forum was held with Labor senator and special envoy for reconciliation Pat Dodson in Ballarat. The audience appeared to be in favour but they were also hungry for the practicalities. \u201cPeople who live in the regions are far more practical,\u201d he told Guardian Australia. \u201cThey want to know how things work. Whereas we\u2019re talking about a principle here, which is significant because it\u2019s about the constitution. In a moral sense and an ethical sense, is this a good thing for us to do?\u201d\nOther campaigns are rolling out, including one from the folks who bought you the Voices For movement \u2013 with a series of kitchen table conversations. Under the banner Together, Yes, these groups are designed to promote quieter, respectful conversations inside homes that fly under the radar of the noisy media debate. The voice process has been a long time in the making. The list of community groups consulted during the co-design phase of the voice is long. Yet for all that consultation, it is clear from the Tamworth meeting that anti-voice forums could get a willing audience in regional areas. While that is sometimes the nature of democracy, the key questions is how voters in 2023 will react to divisive politics and how politicians write their own legacies.", "attributes": {}, "published": </figure>", "body": "<p>The Australian government has banned the use of Chinese social media app TikTok on government devices, following similar bans in other western countries.</p> <p>The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he had authorised the secretary of his department to issue a mandatory direction to prohibit the TikTok app on devices issued by commonwealth departments and agencies.</p> <p>\u201cThe direction will come into effect as soon as practicable,\u201d he said. \u201cExemptions will only be granted on a case-by-case basis and with appropriate security mitigations in place.\u201d</p> <p>According to the <a href=\"https://www.protectivesecurity.gov.au/system/files/2023-04/direction-on-tiktok-application.pdf\">protective security direction</a> issued with the ban, the government states TikTok \u201cposes significant security and privacy risks to non-corporate Commonwealth entities arising from extensive collection of user data and exposure to extrajudicial directions from a foreign government that conflict with Australian law.\u201d</p> <p>Last month the government received a review from the Department of Home Affairs on the security risks of all social media platforms and the correct government settings.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/mar/31/tiktok-why-the-app-with-1bn-users-faces-a-fight-for-its-existence\">TikTok: why the app with 1bn users faces a fight for its existence</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Over half of all federal government agencies currently ban TikTok from government devices, but the overarching policy will ensure consistency across the government.</p> <p>The states and territories were advised of the planned ban on Monday.</p> <p>A Victorian government spokesperson said it would work to adopt the new restrictions on the use of TikTok, while a New South Wales government spokesperson said Cyber Security NSW would continue discussions with the federal government on the new position.</p> <p>TikTok was not informed about the proposed ban prior to it being leaked to media, the company\u2019s Australia and New Zealand general manager Lee Hunter said. He said in a statement that TikTok was \u201cextremely disappointed\u201d by the decision, which the company said was \u201cdriven by politics, not fact\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cAgain, we stress that there is no evidence to suggest that TikTok is in any way a security risk to Australians,\u201d he said, adding that it should not be treated differently to other social media platforms.</p> <p>\u201cOur millions of Australian users deserve a government which makes decisions based upon facts and who treats all businesses fairly, regardless of country of origin.\u201d</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>The shadow cybersecurity minister, James Paterson, told Sky News the government had been \u201cvery very late\u201d in implementing the ban on government devices.</p> <p>The federal opposition had long been calling for the government to act, despite the Coalition failing to do so when in government less than a year ago. Paterson said most of the revelations around security concerns with TikTok and the subsequent government device bans by governments including the US, the UK, Canada and New Zealand only occurred in the past few months.</p> <p>Paterson indicated the opposition would now push for a review on the use of TikTok on personal devices including opening up the possibility of a national ban \u2013<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/mar/27/us-tiktok-ban-aoc-joins-protest\"> something the United States is currently considering.</a></p> <p>\u201cIt\u2019s good that it\u2019s going to be banned from government devices because it removes that espionage risk to public servants,\u201d he said. \u201cBut the data privacy and security risks and also the foreign interference risks that affect millions of Australians using the platform are so far and yet not to be dealt with and they have to be dealt with.\u201d</p> <p>He said that an outright ban of TikTok should be on the table because it could potentially force TikTok\u2019s parent company ByteDance to consider divesting the company from Chinese control.</p> <p>Several MPs, including environment minister Tanya Plibersek and Labor MP Julian Hill, still use TikTok on nongovernment devices. Paterson said he does not use the app, and government services minister Bill Shorten and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/live/2023/apr/04/australia-news-live-tiktok-ban-reserve-bank-interest-rates-phillip-lowe-mortgage-stress-medicare-review?page=with:block-642b6e8c8f08b958e74b1380#block-642b6e8c8f08b958e74b1380\">Victorian premier Daniel Andrews announced on Tuesday </a>they would stop using TikTok.</p> <p>But Greens senator David Shoebridge said the ban wasn\u2019t a serious reform because its focus was too limited.</p> <p>\u201cIf we\u2019re not careful it will just be the first hammer blow in an endless game of online whack-a-mole,\u201d he said. \u201cWe\u2019re in a data security and privacy crisis and we\u2019re fixated on one platform.</p> <p>\u201cThe data security issues for TikTok are mirrored in pretty much every other social media platform, the difference is that our government is not running a fear campaign against the governments that host those platforms.\u201d</p>", "wordcount": "698", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T01:57:23Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T03:18:13Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nkqax", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/b116d95ae2e688dff1ffdeeec3dde02c64a73b22/313_0_7453_4477/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "The Australian government has banned the use of Chinese social media app TikTok on government devices, following similar bans in other western countries. The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he had authorised the secretary of his department to issue a mandatory direction to prohibit the TikTok app on devices issued by commonwealth departments and agencies. \u201cThe direction will come into effect as soon as practicable,\u201d he said. \u201cExemptions will only be granted on a case-by-case basis and with appropriate security mitigations in place.\u201d According to the protective security direction issued with the ban, the government states TikTok \u201cposes significant security and privacy risks to non-corporate Commonwealth entities arising from extensive collection of user data and exposure to extrajudicial directions from a foreign government that conflict with Australian law.\u201d Last month the government received a review from the Department of Home Affairs on the security risks of all social media platforms and the correct government settings. Over half of all federal government agencies currently ban TikTok from government devices, but the overarching policy will ensure consistency across the government. The states and territories were advised of the planned ban on Monday. A Victorian government spokesperson said it would work to adopt the new restrictions on the use of TikTok, while a New South Wales government spokesperson said Cyber Security NSW would continue discussions with the federal government on the new position. TikTok was not informed about the proposed ban prior to it being leaked to media, the company\u2019s Australia and New Zealand general manager Lee Hunter said. He said in a statement that TikTok was \u201cextremely disappointed\u201d by the decision, which the company said was \u201cdriven by politics, not fact\u201d. \u201cAgain, we stress that there is no evidence to suggest that TikTok is in any way a security risk to Australians,\u201d he said, adding that it should not be treated differently to other social media platforms. \u201cOur millions of Australian users deserve a government which makes decisions based upon facts and who treats all businesses fairly, regardless of country of origin.\u201d Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup The shadow cybersecurity minister, James Paterson, told Sky News the government had been \u201cvery very late\u201d in implementing the ban on government devices. It\u2019s time we had systemic change | Peter Lewis", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/commentisfree/2023/apr/04/theres-nothing-natural-about-australias-cost-of-living-crisis-its-time-we-had-systemic-change", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/australia-news/commentisfree/2023/apr/04/theres-nothing-natural-about-australias-cost-of-living-crisis-its-time-we-had-systemic-change", "fields": {"headline": "There\u2019s nothing natural about Australia\u2019s \u2018cost-of-living crisis\u2019. It\u2019s time we had systemic change", "standfirst": "<p>The pain households are feeling right now is the direct consequence of decisions that governments have made over many years</p><ul><li><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/guardian-essential-poll-majority-of-australians-favour-capping-power-prices-lifting-wages-and-cheaper-childcare\">Guardian Essential poll: majority of Australians favour capping power prices, lifting wages and cheaper childcare</a></li></ul>", "trailText": "The pain households are feeling right now is the direct consequence of decisions that governments have made over many years", "byline": "Peter Lewis", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5b4b395141ecefc7d1dd1bf554910c978b52236c\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5b4b395141ecefc7d1dd1bf554910c978b52236c/0_161_5568_3341/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Thinking of the current problem as a \u201ccrisis\u201d makes the moment seem transitory, bushfire to be weathered while absolving government of longer-term accountability for systemic failures,\u2019 writes Peter Lewis.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Lukas Coch/AAP</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has ushered in a feverish cycle of retail politics, where our elected representatives strive to prove both their empathy and utility by promising to end the hip pocket squeeze being felt by many Australians.</p> <p>The Albanese government has made history winning a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/01/aston-byelection-labor-achieves-once-in-a-century-victory-capturing-liberal-heartland-seat\">cost-of-living byelection</a>; New South Wales <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/27/nsw-election-result-deeply-rooted-in-western-sydneys-cost-of-living-crisis\">has changed power</a> in a contest where the hip pocket nerve was vigorously massaged; the upcoming federal budget will inevitably be framed around the issue as well.</p> <p>But there is a wilful blindness at the core of this crisis that risks diminishing the challenges people face by reducing them to a crude consumer price tag.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/guardian-essential-poll-majority-of-australians-favour-capping-power-prices-lifting-wages-and-cheaper-childcare\">Guardian Essential poll: majority of Australians favour capping power prices, lifting wages and cheaper childcare</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has the vibe of a construct cooked up in one of those libertarian language labs that have shifted \u201cglobal warming\u201d to the more benign \u201cclimate change\u201d, \u201cpublic funding\u201d to the fiscally medicinal \u201ctax relief\u201d and \u201cprivatisation\u201d to \u201casset recycling\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cCost of living\u201d implies an inevitability to our current situation, as if this inflationary cycle is just a natural consequence of our worldly existence, part of the rhythm of our economic life that we need to accept.</p> <p>But there is nothing natural about the pain that households are currently feeling. It is the direct consequence of \u201ceconomically rational\u201d decisions (another triumph of rightwing framing) made by governments over many years.</p> <p>There is nothing natural about a housing system that privileges those who have accumulated capital over those who have not; nothing natural about the retail markets that dominate education, nothing natural about our unhealthy reliance on fossil fuels.</p> <p>As <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/guardian-essential-poll-majority-of-australians-favour-capping-power-prices-lifting-wages-and-cheaper-childcare\">this week\u2019s Guardian Essential poll</a> illustrates, these are decisions that are now undermining the economic security of many Australian households</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1VpZNoE1h-YmEqE1dzpCBsYZNS6re_dXqM7M-vzMG3RI\" data-alt=\"For each of the following, please indicate whether you are comfortably able to afford it, finding it a bit difficult to afford or really struggling to afford it.\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1VpZNoE1h-YmEqE1dzpCBsYZNS6re_dXqM7M-vzMG3RI\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>If cost of living is the question, the answer is a resounding \u201couch\u201d. One quarter of the population are doing it tough, another 40% are starting to feel the heat, leaving just a third in the comfort of the economic banana chair.</p> <p>This speaks to a second problem with the \u201ccost-of-living\u201d frame. It vests the challenge to find the money to pay the bills and mortgage in the individual, rather than seeing these as the inevitable consequence of turning essential services into private markets and transforming citizens into customers.</p> <p>As individual consumers it\u2019s on us to navigate the system, with the government\u2019s role confined to improving the flow of consumer information. Remember the Rudd government\u2019s impotent response to post-GFC rising prices with a string of price comparison websites?</p> <p>Finally, thinking of the current problem as a \u201ccrisis\u201d makes the moment seem transitory, more like an economic storm or bushfire to be weathered while absolving government of longer-term accountability for systemic failures.</p> <p>But a separate question suggests most people have not read the free market memo and expect the government to intervene with more than crisis payments and emergency accommodation.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1RUl59s7XOJoBHdT8KFI3aMT2gqhkTPXxTTQs8-IhSpQ\" data-alt=\"How much difference do you think the federal government could make to the rising cost of living?\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1RUl59s7XOJoBHdT8KFI3aMT2gqhkTPXxTTQs8-IhSpQ\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>People, especially the young who are most affected by the impacts of inflation, believe government can \u2013 or more appositely could \u2013 make a difference if they chose to. It\u2019s what politicians do with that expectation that is more revealing.</p> <p>Ironically, for the conservative political parties that have always governed in the name of \u201cfree markets\u201d, blaming the new government for living costs requires a suspension of disbelief that not even the Murdoch echo chamber can sustain.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/17/guardian-readers-share-their-ideas-and-hints-for-saving-money-in-these-difficult-times\">Guardian readers share their ideas and hints for saving money in these difficult times | First Dog on the Moon</a> </p> </aside>  <p>This was borne out in the Coalition\u2019s abject failure in the Aston byelection over the weekend, where the federal Coalition discovered that simply shouting \u201ccost of living\u201d is not enough to convince people you have a plan.</p> <p>Meanwhile in NSW, Labor\u2019s successful prosecution of the \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, a key issue in winning minority government, was more a series of Band-Aids placed on existing policy wounds than transformative change.</p> <p>NSW Labor promised to lift the 2.5% public sector wages cap designed to peg pay claims to inflation but now delivering declining living standards to nurses, teachers and many other tens of thousands who work directly for the government.</p> <p>It also pledged to end the Coalition\u2019s privatisation fetish and specifically vowed to keep Sydney Water, one of the few assets that hadn\u2019t been flogged off already, in public hands.</p> <p>Most gallingly, it promised to redirect significant taxpayer dollars to offset the exorbitant tolls multinational corporations are charging people to get around a city too reliant on private roads. Cost of living, indeed.</p> <p>A final table shows there is an appetite for government to take the discussion beyond the sticker and open a more honest discussion on the very architecture of our economy.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1DOMqtP2m0p-NGojfeFhimq1AtvYraxOMeKpZ8rXQWnQ\" data-alt=\"Here is a list of things the government could try to deal with the rising cost of living. For each of the following, please indicate if it\u2019s:\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1DOMqtP2m0p-NGojfeFhimq1AtvYraxOMeKpZ8rXQWnQ\">Interactive</a> <figcaption>For each of the following, please indicate whether you are comfortably able to afford it, finding it a bit difficult to afford or really struggling to afford it.</figcaption> </figure>  <p>These figures show the depth of public appetite for government intervention into the markets of essential services, not just at the point of sale, but at the source.</p> <p>Ideas that have been off the table for the best part of four decades including centrally mandated price caps on energy prices enjoy overwhelming public support. Tax cuts are also as popular as ever, highlighting the challenges in reversing even the regressive stage-three handouts to the wealthy.</p> <p>But it is support for systemic change that also stands out, starting with the shambolic childcare market that has placed unnecessary economic stress on young families while placing unfair barriers on women returning to paid employment.</p> <p>Turning this failing market into a coherent, universal early learning system will ease price pressure, increase immediate productivity and invest in kids for the long term.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/27/sole-parents-like-myself-are-at-the-frontline-of-the-cost-of-living-crisis-will-albanese-right-the-wrongs-of-his-predecessors\">Sole parents like me are at the frontline of the cost-of-living crisis. Will Albanese right the wrongs of his predecessors? | Genevieve O\u2019Connell</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Speaking of working, the workplace minister, Tony Burke, has recognised one of the huge drivers of cost-of-living pressure is at the input end, a bargaining system designed to minimise wages for labour and maximise profits to capital.</p> <p>Even before our current \u201ccrisis\u201d, the Reserve Bank was warning of the impact of artificially low wages. Now it is these same Paye wage-earners who are facing the brunt of rising prices who are being most squeezed.</p> <p>There is strong support in our Guardian Essential poll for Burke\u2019s endorsement of a higher minimum wage; but this is not the only work on his agenda to get wages moving, with \u201csame jobs, same pay\u201d laws to increase pay for labour hours and contractors.</p> <p>Rather than being in a \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, maybe we are actually in an \u201cinequality crisis\u201d or a \u201cmarkets charging us too much (while continuing to deliver massive profits) crisis\u201d.</p> <p>Or maybe we are entering a \u201cfinally taking responsibility for the way our nation functions\u201d moment. Less \u201ccost of living\u201d, more \u201cchoice of how we live together\u201d.</p> <p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d narrative served Labor well in opposition, allowing it to shift the abstract economic management contest to the kitchen table.</p> <p>Moving beyond it in power could give it its best chance to create the momentum and urgency to deliver on its broader policy agenda and render the Coalition, which will instinctively oppose anything it does, even more irrelevant.</p> <ul> <li><p>Peter Lewis is an executive director of Essential, a progressive strategic communications and research company</p></li> </ul>", "wordcount": "1127", "commentCloseDate": "2023-04-07T01:03:32Z", "commentable": "true", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T01:03:32Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T04:56:30Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nkpm4", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/5b4b395141ecefc7d1dd1bf554910c978b52236c/0_161_5568_3341/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has ushered in a feverish cycle of retail politics, where our elected representatives strive to prove both their empathy and utility by promising to end the hip pocket squeeze being felt by many Australians. The Albanese government has made history winning a cost-of-living byelection; New South Wales has changed power in a contest where the hip pocket nerve was vigorously massaged; the upcoming federal budget will inevitably be framed around the issue as well. But there is a wilful blindness at the core of this crisis that risks diminishing the challenges people face by reducing them to a crude consumer price tag. The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has the vibe of a construct cooked up in one of those libertarian language labs that have shifted \u201cglobal warming\u201d to the more benign \u201cclimate change\u201d, \u201cpublic funding\u201d to the fiscally medicinal \u201ctax relief\u201d and \u201cprivatisation\u201d to \u201casset recycling\u201d. \u201cCost of living\u201d implies an inevitability to our current situation, as if this inflationary cycle is just a natural consequence of our worldly existence, part of the rhythm of our economic life that we need to accept. But there is nothing natural about the pain that households are currently feeling. It is the direct consequence of \u201ceconomically rational\u201d decisions (another triumph of rightwing framing) made by governments over many years. There is nothing natural about a housing system that privileges those who have accumulated capital over those who have not; nothing natural about the retail markets that dominate education, nothing natural about our unhealthy reliance on fossil fuels. As this week\u2019s Guardian Essential poll illustrates, these are decisions that are now undermining the economic security of many Australian households If cost of living is the question, the answer is a resounding \u201couch\u201d. One quarter of the population are doing it tough, another 40% are starting to feel the heat, leaving just a third in the comfort of the economic banana chair. This speaks to a second problem with the \u201ccost-of-living\u201d frame. It vests the challenge to find the money to pay the bills and mortgage in the individual, rather than seeing these as the inevitable consequence of turning essential services into private markets and transforming citizens into customers. As individual consumers it\u2019s on us to navigate the system, with the government\u2019s role confined to improving the flow of consumer information. Remember the Rudd government\u2019s impotent response to post-GFC rising prices with a string of price comparison websites? Finally, thinking of the current problem as a \u201ccrisis\u201d makes the moment seem transitory, more like an economic storm or bushfire to be weathered while absolving government of longer-term accountability for systemic failures. But a separate question suggests most people have not read the free market memo and expect the government to intervene with more than crisis payments and emergency accommodation. People, especially the young who are most affected by the impacts of inflation, believe government can \u2013 or more appositely could \u2013 make a difference if they chose to. It\u2019s what politicians do with that expectation that is more revealing. Ironically, for the conservative political parties that have always governed in the name of \u201cfree markets\u201d, blaming the new government for living costs requires a suspension of disbelief that not even the Murdoch echo chamber can sustain. This was borne out in the Coalition\u2019s abject failure in the Aston byelection over the weekend, where the federal Coalition discovered that simply shouting \u201ccost of living\u201d is not enough to convince people you have a plan. Meanwhile in NSW, Labor\u2019s successful prosecution of the \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, a key issue in winning minority government, was more a series of Band-Aids placed on existing policy wounds than transformative change. NSW Labor promised to lift the 2.5% public sector wages cap designed to peg pay claims to inflation but now delivering declining living standards to nurses, teachers and many other tens of thousands who work directly for the government. It also pledged to end the Coalition\u2019s privatisation fetish and specifically vowed to keep Sydney Water, one of the few assets that hadn\u2019t been flogged off already, in public hands. Most gallingly, it promised to redirect significant taxpayer dollars to offset the exorbitant tolls multinational corporations are charging people to get around a city too reliant on private roads. Cost of living, indeed. A final table shows there is an appetite for government to take the discussion beyond the sticker and open a more honest discussion on the very architecture of our economy. These figures show the depth of public appetite for government intervention into the markets of essential services, not just at the point of sale, but at the source. Ideas that have been off the table for the best part of four decades including centrally mandated price caps on energy prices enjoy overwhelming public support. Tax cuts are also as popular as ever, highlighting the challenges in reversing even the regressive stage-three handouts to the wealthy. But it is support for systemic change that also stands out, starting with the shambolic childcare market that has placed unnecessary economic stress on young families while placing unfair barriers on women returning to paid employment. Turning this failing market into a coherent, universal early learning system will ease price pressure, increase immediate productivity and invest in kids for the long term. Speaking of working, the workplace minister, Tony Burke, has recognised one of the huge drivers of cost-of-living pressure is at the input end, a bargaining system designed to minimise wages for labour and maximise profits to capital. Even before our current \u201ccrisis\u201d, the Reserve Bank was warning of the impact of artificially low wages. Now it is these same Paye wage-earners who are facing the brunt of rising prices who are being most squeezed. There is strong support in our Guardian Essential poll for Burke\u2019s endorsement of a higher minimum wage; but this is not the only work on his agenda to get wages moving, with \u201csame jobs, same pay\u201d laws to increase pay for labour hours and contractors. Rather than being in a \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, maybe we are actually in an \u201cinequality crisis\u201d or a \u201cmarkets charging us too much (while continuing to deliver massive profits) crisis\u201d. Or maybe we are entering a \u201cfinally taking responsibility for the way our nation functions\u201d moment. Less \u201ccost of living\u201d, more \u201cchoice of how we live together\u201d. The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d narrative served Labor well in opposition, allowing it to shift the abstract economic management contest to the kitchen table. Moving beyond it in power could give it its best chance to create the momentum and urgency to deliver on its broader policy agenda and render the Coalition, which will instinctively oppose anything it does, even more irrelevant. "Photograph"}}]}, "body": [{"id": "642b467d8f08b958e74b1270", "bodyHtml": "<p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has ushered in a feverish cycle of retail politics, where our elected representatives strive to prove both their empathy and utility by promising to end the hip pocket squeeze being felt by many Australians.</p> <p>The Albanese government has made history winning a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/01/aston-byelection-labor-achieves-once-in-a-century-victory-capturing-liberal-heartland-seat\">cost-of-living byelection</a>; New South Wales <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/27/nsw-election-result-deeply-rooted-in-western-sydneys-cost-of-living-crisis\">has changed power</a> in a contest where the hip pocket nerve was vigorously massaged; the upcoming federal budget will inevitably be framed around the issue as well.</p> <p>But there is a wilful blindness at the core of this crisis that risks diminishing the challenges people face by reducing them to a crude consumer price tag.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/guardian-essential-poll-majority-of-australians-favour-capping-power-prices-lifting-wages-and-cheaper-childcare\">Guardian Essential poll: majority of Australians favour capping power prices, lifting wages and cheaper childcare</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has the vibe of a construct cooked up in one of those libertarian language labs that have shifted \u201cglobal warming\u201d to the more benign \u201cclimate change\u201d, \u201cpublic funding\u201d to the fiscally medicinal \u201ctax relief\u201d and \u201cprivatisation\u201d to \u201casset recycling\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cCost of living\u201d implies an inevitability to our current situation, as if this inflationary cycle is just a natural consequence of our worldly existence, part of the rhythm of our economic life that we need to accept.</p> <p>But there is nothing natural about the pain that households are currently feeling. It is the direct consequence of \u201ceconomically rational\u201d decisions (another triumph of rightwing framing) made by governments over many years.</p> <p>There is nothing natural about a housing system that privileges those who have accumulated capital over those who have not; nothing natural about the retail markets that dominate education, nothing natural about our unhealthy reliance on fossil fuels.</p> <p>As <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/04/guardian-essential-poll-majority-of-australians-favour-capping-power-prices-lifting-wages-and-cheaper-childcare\">this week\u2019s Guardian Essential poll</a> illustrates, these are decisions that are now undermining the economic security of many Australian households</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1VpZNoE1h-YmEqE1dzpCBsYZNS6re_dXqM7M-vzMG3RI\" data-alt=\"For each of the following, please indicate whether you are comfortably able to afford it, finding it a bit difficult to afford or really struggling to afford it.\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1VpZNoE1h-YmEqE1dzpCBsYZNS6re_dXqM7M-vzMG3RI\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>If cost of living is the question, the answer is a resounding \u201couch\u201d. One quarter of the population are doing it tough, another 40% are starting to feel the heat, leaving just a third in the comfort of the economic banana chair.</p> <p>This speaks to a second problem with the \u201ccost-of-living\u201d frame. It vests the challenge to find the money to pay the bills and mortgage in the individual, rather than seeing these as the inevitable consequence of turning essential services into private markets and transforming citizens into customers.</p> <p>As individual consumers it\u2019s on us to navigate the system, with the government\u2019s role confined to improving the flow of consumer information. Remember the Rudd government\u2019s impotent response to post-GFC rising prices with a string of price comparison websites?</p> <p>Finally, thinking of the current problem as a \u201ccrisis\u201d makes the moment seem transitory, more like an economic storm or bushfire to be weathered while absolving government of longer-term accountability for systemic failures.</p> <p>But a separate question suggests most people have not read the free market memo and expect the government to intervene with more than crisis payments and emergency accommodation.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1RUl59s7XOJoBHdT8KFI3aMT2gqhkTPXxTTQs8-IhSpQ\" data-alt=\"How much difference do you think the federal government could make to the rising cost of living?\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1RUl59s7XOJoBHdT8KFI3aMT2gqhkTPXxTTQs8-IhSpQ\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>People, especially the young who are most affected by the impacts of inflation, believe government can \u2013 or more appositely could \u2013 make a difference if they chose to. It\u2019s what politicians do with that expectation that is more revealing.</p> <p>Ironically, for the conservative political parties that have always governed in the name of \u201cfree markets\u201d, blaming the new government for living costs requires a suspension of disbelief that not even the Murdoch echo chamber can sustain.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/17/guardian-readers-share-their-ideas-and-hints-for-saving-money-in-these-difficult-times\">Guardian readers share their ideas and hints for saving money in these difficult times | First Dog on the Moon</a> </p> </aside>  <p>This was borne out in the Coalition\u2019s abject failure in the Aston byelection over the weekend, where the federal Coalition discovered that simply shouting \u201ccost of living\u201d is not enough to convince people you have a plan.</p> <p>Meanwhile in NSW, Labor\u2019s successful prosecution of the \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, a key issue in winning minority government, was more a series of Band-Aids placed on existing policy wounds than transformative change.</p> <p>NSW Labor promised to lift the 2.5% public sector wages cap designed to peg pay claims to inflation but now delivering declining living standards to nurses, teachers and many other tens of thousands who work directly for the government.</p> <p>It also pledged to end the Coalition\u2019s privatisation fetish and specifically vowed to keep Sydney Water, one of the few assets that hadn\u2019t been flogged off already, in public hands.</p> <p>Most gallingly, it promised to redirect significant taxpayer dollars to offset the exorbitant tolls multinational corporations are charging people to get around a city too reliant on private roads. Cost of living, indeed.</p> <p>A final table shows there is an appetite for government to take the discussion beyond the sticker and open a more honest discussion on the very architecture of our economy.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1DOMqtP2m0p-NGojfeFhimq1AtvYraxOMeKpZ8rXQWnQ\" data-alt=\"Here is a list of things the government could try to deal with the rising cost of living. For each of the following, please indicate if it\u2019s:\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/atoms/2020/08/table-tool/embed/app/main.html?spreadsheet=1DOMqtP2m0p-NGojfeFhimq1AtvYraxOMeKpZ8rXQWnQ\">Interactive</a> <figcaption>For each of the following, please indicate whether you are comfortably able to afford it, finding it a bit difficult to afford or really struggling to afford it.</figcaption> </figure>  <p>These figures show the depth of public appetite for government intervention into the markets of essential services, not just at the point of sale, but at the source.</p> <p>Ideas that have been off the table for the best part of four decades including centrally mandated price caps on energy prices enjoy overwhelming public support. Tax cuts are also as popular as ever, highlighting the challenges in reversing even the regressive stage-three handouts to the wealthy.</p> <p>But it is support for systemic change that also stands out, starting with the shambolic childcare market that has placed unnecessary economic stress on young families while placing unfair barriers on women returning to paid employment.</p> <p>Turning this failing market into a coherent, universal early learning system will ease price pressure, increase immediate productivity and invest in kids for the long term.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/27/sole-parents-like-myself-are-at-the-frontline-of-the-cost-of-living-crisis-will-albanese-right-the-wrongs-of-his-predecessors\">Sole parents like me are at the frontline of the cost-of-living crisis. Will Albanese right the wrongs of his predecessors? | Genevieve O\u2019Connell</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Speaking of working, the workplace minister, Tony Burke, has recognised one of the huge drivers of cost-of-living pressure is at the input end, a bargaining system designed to minimise wages for labour and maximise profits to capital.</p> <p>Even before our current \u201ccrisis\u201d, the Reserve Bank was warning of the impact of artificially low wages. Now it is these same Paye wage-earners who are facing the brunt of rising prices who are being most squeezed.</p> <p>There is strong support in our Guardian Essential poll for Burke\u2019s endorsement of a higher minimum wage; but this is not the only work on his agenda to get wages moving, with \u201csame jobs, same pay\u201d laws to increase pay for labour hours and contractors.</p> <p>Rather than being in a \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, maybe we are actually in an \u201cinequality crisis\u201d or a \u201cmarkets charging us too much (while continuing to deliver massive profits) crisis\u201d.</p> <p>Or maybe we are entering a \u201cfinally taking responsibility for the way our nation functions\u201d moment. Less \u201ccost of living\u201d, more \u201cchoice of how we live together\u201d.</p> <p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d narrative served Labor well in opposition, allowing it to shift the abstract economic management contest to the kitchen table.</p> <p>Moving beyond it in power could give it its best chance to create the momentum and urgency to deliver on its broader policy agenda and render the Coalition, which will instinctively oppose anything it does, even more irrelevant.</p> <ul> <li><p>Peter Lewis is an executive director of Essential, a progressive strategic communications and research company</p></li> </ul>", "bodyTextSummary": "The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has ushered in a feverish cycle of retail politics, where our elected representatives strive to prove both their empathy and utility by promising to end the hip pocket squeeze being felt by many Australians. The Albanese government has made history winning a cost-of-living byelection; New South Wales has changed power in a contest where the hip pocket nerve was vigorously massaged; the upcoming federal budget will inevitably be framed around the issue as well. But there is a wilful blindness at the core of this crisis that risks diminishing the challenges people face by reducing them to a crude consumer price tag.\nThe \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d has the vibe of a construct cooked up in one of those libertarian language labs that have shifted \u201cglobal warming\u201d to the more benign \u201cclimate change\u201d, \u201cpublic funding\u201d to the fiscally medicinal \u201ctax relief\u201d and \u201cprivatisation\u201d to \u201casset recycling\u201d. \u201cCost of living\u201d implies an inevitability to our current situation, as if this inflationary cycle is just a natural consequence of our worldly existence, part of the rhythm of our economic life that we need to accept. But there is nothing natural about the pain that households are currently feeling. It is the direct consequence of \u201ceconomically rational\u201d decisions (another triumph of rightwing framing) made by governments over many years. There is nothing natural about a housing system that privileges those who have accumulated capital over those who have not; nothing natural about the retail markets that dominate education, nothing natural about our unhealthy reliance on fossil fuels. As this week\u2019s Guardian Essential poll illustrates, these are decisions that are now undermining the economic security of many Australian households\nIf cost of living is the question, the answer is a resounding \u201couch\u201d. One quarter of the population are doing it tough, another 40% are starting to feel the heat, leaving just a third in the comfort of the economic banana chair. This speaks to a second problem with the \u201ccost-of-living\u201d frame. It vests the challenge to find the money to pay the bills and mortgage in the individual, rather than seeing these as the inevitable consequence of turning essential services into private markets and transforming citizens into customers. As individual consumers it\u2019s on us to navigate the system, with the government\u2019s role confined to improving the flow of consumer information. Remember the Rudd government\u2019s impotent response to post-GFC rising prices with a string of price comparison websites? Finally, thinking of the current problem as a \u201ccrisis\u201d makes the moment seem transitory, more like an economic storm or bushfire to be weathered while absolving government of longer-term accountability for systemic failures. But a separate question suggests most people have not read the free market memo and expect the government to intervene with more than crisis payments and emergency accommodation.\nPeople, especially the young who are most affected by the impacts of inflation, believe government can \u2013 or more appositely could \u2013 make a difference if they chose to. It\u2019s what politicians do with that expectation that is more revealing. Ironically, for the conservative political parties that have always governed in the name of \u201cfree markets\u201d, blaming the new government for living costs requires a suspension of disbelief that not even the Murdoch echo chamber can sustain.\nThis was borne out in the Coalition\u2019s abject failure in the Aston byelection over the weekend, where the federal Coalition discovered that simply shouting \u201ccost of living\u201d is not enough to convince people you have a plan. Meanwhile in NSW, Labor\u2019s successful prosecution of the \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, a key issue in winning minority government, was more a series of Band-Aids placed on existing policy wounds than transformative change. NSW Labor promised to lift the 2.5% public sector wages cap designed to peg pay claims to inflation but now delivering declining living standards to nurses, teachers and many other tens of thousands who work directly for the government. It also pledged to end the Coalition\u2019s privatisation fetish and specifically vowed to keep Sydney Water, one of the few assets that hadn\u2019t been flogged off already, in public hands. Most gallingly, it promised to redirect significant taxpayer dollars to offset the exorbitant tolls multinational corporations are charging people to get around a city too reliant on private roads. Cost of living, indeed. A final table shows there is an appetite for government to take the discussion beyond the sticker and open a more honest discussion on the very architecture of our economy.\nThese figures show the depth of public appetite for government intervention into the markets of essential services, not just at the point of sale, but at the source. Ideas that have been off the table for the best part of four decades including centrally mandated price caps on energy prices enjoy overwhelming public support. Tax cuts are also as popular as ever, highlighting the challenges in reversing even the regressive stage-three handouts to the wealthy. But it is support for systemic change that also stands out, starting with the shambolic childcare market that has placed unnecessary economic stress on young families while placing unfair barriers on women returning to paid employment. Turning this failing market into a coherent, universal early learning system will ease price pressure, increase immediate productivity and invest in kids for the long term.\nSpeaking of working, the workplace minister, Tony Burke, has recognised one of the huge drivers of cost-of-living pressure is at the input end, a bargaining system designed to minimise wages for labour and maximise profits to capital. Even before our current \u201ccrisis\u201d, the Reserve Bank was warning of the impact of artificially low wages. Now it is these same Paye wage-earners who are facing the brunt of rising prices who are being most squeezed. There is strong support in our Guardian Essential poll for Burke\u2019s endorsement of a higher minimum wage; but this is not the only work on his agenda to get wages moving, with \u201csame jobs, same pay\u201d laws to increase pay for labour hours and contractors. Rather than being in a \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, maybe we are actually in an \u201cinequality crisis\u201d or a \u201cmarkets charging us too much (while continuing to deliver massive profits) crisis\u201d. Or maybe we are entering a \u201cfinally taking responsibility for the way our nation functions\u201d moment. Less \u201ccost of living\u201d, more \u201cchoice of how we live together\u201d. The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d narrative served Labor well in opposition, allowing it to shift the abstract economic management contest to the kitchen table. Moving beyond it in power could give it its best chance to create the momentum and urgency to deliver on its broader policy agenda and render the Coalition, which will instinctively oppose anything it does, even more irrelevant. Tax cuts are also as popular as ever, highlighting the challenges in reversing even the regressive stage-three handouts to the wealthy.</p> \n<p>But it is support for systemic change that also stands out, starting with the shambolic childcare market that has placed unnecessary economic stress on young families while placing unfair barriers on women returning to paid employment.</p> \n<p>Turning this failing market into a coherent, universal early learning system will ease price pressure, increase immediate productivity and invest in kids for the long term.</p>"}}, {"type": "rich-link", "assets": [], "richLinkTypeData": {"url": "https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/27/sole-parents-like-myself-are-at-the-frontline-of-the-cost-of-living-crisis-will-albanese-right-the-wrongs-of-his-predecessors", "originalUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/27/sole-parents-like-myself-are-at-the-frontline-of-the-cost-of-living-crisis-will-albanese-right-the-wrongs-of-his-predecessors", "linkText": "Sole parents like me are at the frontline of the cost-of-living crisis. Now it is these same Paye wage-earners who are facing the brunt of rising prices who are being most squeezed.</p> \n<p>There is strong support in our Guardian Essential poll for Burke\u2019s endorsement of a higher minimum wage; but this is not the only work on his agenda to get wages moving, with \u201csame jobs, same pay\u201d laws to increase pay for labour hours and contractors.</p> \n<p>Rather than being in a \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d, maybe we are actually in an \u201cinequality crisis\u201d or a \u201cmarkets charging us too much (while continuing to deliver massive profits) crisis\u201d.</p> \n<p>Or maybe we are entering a \u201cfinally taking responsibility for the way our nation functions\u201d moment. Less \u201ccost of living\u201d, more \u201cchoice of how we live together\u201d.</p> \n<p>The \u201ccost-of-living crisis\u201d narrative served Labor well in opposition, allowing it to shift the abstract economic management contest to the kitchen table.</p> \n<p>Moving beyond it in power could give it its best chance to create the momentum and urgency to deliver on its broader policy agenda
