"https://content.guardianapis.com/world/2023/mar/30/asia-pacific-flashpoints-fuelling-regional-arms-race-taiwan-north-korea-south-china-sea-pacific-islands", "fields": {"headline": "Asia\u2019s arms race: potential flashpoints from Taiwan to the South China Sea", "standfirst": "<p>Guardian correspondents look at the buildup of tensions across the region, from North Korea to the Pacific islands</p>", "trailText": "Guardian correspondents look at the buildup of tensions across the region, from North Korea to the Pacific islands", "byline": "Amy Hawkins, Helen Davidson, Justin McCurry, Rebecca Ratcliffe and Daniel Hurst", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"e1c9cd91c6d02378c55e5ec36ed1269aa948a472\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/e1c9cd91c6d02378c55e5ec36ed1269aa948a472/0_66_2000_1201/1000.jpg\" alt=\"South Korean marines make a beach landing during a joint US-Philippine military exercise northwest of Manila\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">South Korean marines make a beach landing during a joint US-Philippine military exercise northwest of Manila</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Basilio Sepe/ZUMA Press Wire/REX/Shutterstock</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>Global militarisation has increased rapidly over the course of the 21st century, with a clear recalibration towards the Asia Pacific. Between 2000 and 2021, annual military spending increased from $1.12trn (in 2020 dollar terms) to $2.11trn. In that time, the share of global defence spending in Asia and Oceania increased from 18% to 28%, according to data compiled by the Stockholm International Peace Research Institute.</p> <p>Growing concerns over China, whose defence spending has increased steadily for nearly three decades, has fuelled an arms race across the region, upending decades-long regional conventions and revealing several potential flashpoints.</p> <h2><strong>Taiwan</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"ec74f5ad404396c2bfcb8a71049fe23f53fd5c69\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/ec74f5ad404396c2bfcb8a71049fe23f53fd5c69/138_440_5209_3125/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Chinese military helicopters take part in military drills following Nancy Pelosi\u2019s visit to Taiwan in 2022.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Chinese military helicopters take part in military drills following Nancy Pelosi\u2019s visit to Taiwan in 2022.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: H\u00e9ctor Retamal/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The biggest concern about China\u2019s military expansion is how it ties in to plans to annex Taiwan. The risk of a Chinese invasion of Taiwan is the highest it\u2019s been in decades, but predictions of when it might happen vary.</p> <p>Recently a number of senior US military leaders have offered <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/02/us-general-gut-feeling-war-china-sparks-alarm-predictions\">alarmingly imminent dates</a> including 2023, 2025 and 2027. American assessments are crucial, given it\u2019s possible the US military will help defend Taiwan against Chinese attacks, but some analysts caution that these military figures are likely exaggerating to lobby for more funding. Sources say the public predictions have frustrated Taiwan\u2019s president, who is trying to ensure people are worried enough about the China threat that they support her policies, but not so much that they give up hope.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/tawian-zip/giv-13425IteVVK9WkF68/\" data-alt=\"Taiwan map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/tawian-zip/giv-13425IteVVK9WkF68/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>However, the scale of China\u2019s military expansion offers analysts some clues to its ambitions for Taiwan.</p> <p>While still spending a smaller share of its GDP on defence than the US, China\u2019s defence budget will increase by 7.2% in 2023, taking it to the highest point it\u2019s ever been. The US Department of Defense estimates that China\u2019s real military spending could be up to twice as much as the officially reported figures. In response, the defence portion of the most recent US budget has prioritised <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/10/biden-budget-includes-billions-for-pacific-islands-in-bid-to-out-compete-china\">ramping up the military presence</a> in the Indo-Pacific. This month the US also approved the potential sale of $619m in new weapons to Taiwan.</p> <p>Just as important as the increased spending is how it affects the balance of military strength in the region. In 2000, China was the second-largest defence spender in the Indo-Pacific. By 2021 it was spending more on defence than the next 13 countries in <a href=\"https://www.csis.org/analysis/what-does-china-really-spend-its-military#:~:text=In%20March%202023%2C%20China%20announced,1.45%20trillion%20(%24229.6%20billion).&amp;text=This%20continues%20a%20recent%20trend,in%20the%20upper%20single%20digits.\">the region combined</a>.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/charts/embed/mar/2023-03-29-01:20:00/embed.html\" data-alt=\"China defence spending chart\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/charts/embed/mar/2023-03-29-01:20:00/embed.html\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>China has also taken great exception to a tripartite agreement between Australia, the US and the UK. The AU$368bn deal, known as Aukus, will see the US and UK help Australia acquire at least three nuclear-powered submarines over the next three decades. Aukus is widely seen as being aimed at countering China\u2019s military expansion, and has drawn mixed reactions. China accused the allies of deception, using loopholes in non-proliferation treaties, and \u201cwalking further and further down the path of error and danger\u201d. Australia\u2019s defence minister responded by saying, \u201cChina invests \u2026 in its own defence capabilities; we are doing that in respect of ours.\u201d</p> <p><em>Helen Davidson in Taipei and Amy Hawkins</em></p> <h2><strong>North Korea</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"17f40379b67cb9986daf2294f97fc639daba485b\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/17f40379b67cb9986daf2294f97fc639daba485b/0_26_2674_1605/1000.jpg\" alt=\"North Korean leader Kim Jong-un inspects various types of warheads designed to be mounted on missiles or rocket launches.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">North Korean leader Kim Jong-un inspects various types of warheads designed to be mounted on missiles or rocket launches.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: \u671d\u9bae\u901a\u4fe1\u793e/AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Having launched about 90 ballistic missiles and other weapons in 2022 \u2013 more than in any other year \u2013 North Korea appears intent on setting a new record this year. It has held <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/19/north-korea-continues-run-of-weapons-tests-with-ballistic-missile-launch\">11 rounds of missile tests</a>, some involving more than one weapon, so far in 2023, including two intercontinental ballistic missiles and, according to state media, strategic cruise missiles with a nuclear capability.</p> <p>In a sign that the regime in Pyongyang has no intention of relinquishing its weapons of mass destruction, it recently unveiled new, smaller nuclear warheads and vowed to produce more weapons-grade nuclear material. Kim Jong-un called for an <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/jan/01/kim-jong-un-north-korea-exponentially-increase-nuclear-warhead-production\">\u201cexponential increase\u201d in the regime\u2019s nuclear arsenal</a> at the end of last year.</p> <p>The North\u2019s provocations are directed towards its nemeses: the US and South Korea. As March drew to a close, forces from both countries engaged in joint maritime exercises that included nuclear-powered aircraft carrier the USS Nimitz, days after they ended their largest joint military exercises in five years. The allies insist the drills are purely defensive in nature, but Pyongyang condemns them as a rehearsal for an invasion.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/n_korea-zip/giv-13425O9qdUjJwar8V/\" data-alt=\"Map of North Korea\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/n_korea-zip/giv-13425O9qdUjJwar8V/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Rising <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/aug/15/north-and-south-korea-tensions-relations-issue-conflict-division-explained-in-30-seconds\">tensions on the Korean peninsula</a> have triggered a hardline response from South Korea\u2019s conservative president, Yoon Suk Yeol, who has warned that Seoul will not provide a \u201csingle penny\u201d to the North as long as it continues to develop nuclear weapons. More worryingly for those eager to dial down tensions, a serious debate is unfolding over whether Seoul should acquire its own nuclear deterrent \u2013 a move a majority of South Koreans support, according to polls.</p> <p>In Japan, concern over North Korea and an increasingly assertive China has boosted the case for steering the country further away from its postwar \u201cpacifism.\u201d Late last year, the government of prime minister Fumio Kishida announced Japan would <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/dec/16/japan-approves-biggest-military-buildup-since-second-world-war-amid-china-fears\">double defence spending to 2% of GDP</a> by 2027, abandoning its self-imposed cap of 1% of GDP.</p> <p>The increasingly unstable security environment in the Asia-Pacific has been the catalyst for a long-awaited thaw in relations between Japan and South Korea \u2013 both US allies with tens of thousands of American troops based on their territory.</p> <p>In March, Yoon and Kishida became the first South Korean and Japanese leaders <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/16/yoon-arrives-in-japan-for-historic-talks-with-kishida-and-beloved-omurice\">to hold bilateral talks for 12 years</a>. Their summit in Tokyo came amid attempts to resolve disputes connected to their bitter wartime legacy, and resulted in an agreement to resume reciprocal visits and a security dialogue after a five-year hiatus.</p> <p><em>Justin McCurry in Tokyo</em></p> <h2><strong>South China Sea</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"f59f55677b28705fbef57c2fd74334af29d784e0\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f59f55677b28705fbef57c2fd74334af29d784e0/0_7_6000_3600/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Philippine troops gather as president Ferdinand Marcos Jr. announces four new military bases, including in a province facing the South China Sea.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Philippine troops gather as president Ferdinand Marcos Jr. announces four new military bases, including in a province facing the South China Sea.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Ezra Acayan/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The South China Sea is one of the most strategically and economically important waterways in the world. It is one of the world\u2019s busiest trading routes, rich in marine life and is thought to contain significant reserves of oil and natural gas. It\u2019s also highly contested.</p> <p>China claims almost all of the South China Sea, with its so-called \u201cnine-dash\u201d line marking the limit of the country\u2019s claim \u2013 though an<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/jul/12/philippines-wins-south-china-sea-case-against-china\"> international tribunal in The Hague has rejected this</a>. The Philippines, as well as Vietnam, Malaysia, Brunei and Taiwan also have overlapping claims. The US, while not a claimant, considers the water crucial to its national interests.</p> <p>Under president Ferdinand Marcos, the Philippines has taken a much tougher stance on the dispute, with Manila accusing Chinese vessels of \u201caggressive actions\u201d in the South China Sea.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/s_china_sea-zip/giv-13425sGxq1YFye1KF/\" data-alt=\"South China Sea map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/s_china_sea-zip/giv-13425sGxq1YFye1KF/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Marcos has vowed the country \u201cwill not lose one inch\u201d of its territory, and has overseen a massive shift in foreign policy, strengthening defence ties with the US and its allies.</p> <p>In March the Philippines <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/18/the-tiny-philippine-island-on-the-frontline-of-the-us-china-battle-for-supremacy\">expanded US access to its military bases</a>, enhancing Washington\u2019s footprint in the region, and enabling it to more easily monitor Chinese activity in the South China Sea near Taiwan. It has also strengthened military ties with Japan and Australia, two US allies.</p> <p>In contrast to neighbouring Southeast Asia countries that have expressed concerns over Aukus, which some nations fear could prompt an arms race that destabilises the region, the Philippines has supported the development.</p> <p>The approach taken by Marcos, who came to power last year, is a stark contrast to that of his predecessor, former president Rodrigo Duterte. Duterte had sought closer economic ties with Beijing and was reluctant to confront China over its activities in the South China sea \u2013 even as China constructed military bases on human-made islands, and Chinese vessels harassed, or rammed, Filippine boats.</p> <p><em>Rebecca Ratcliffe in Bangkok</em></p> <h2><strong>Pacific Islands</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"fa289ea88434fe42838636b225c0af17de597362\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/fa289ea88434fe42838636b225c0af17de597362/0_332_5000_3001/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Former Chinese premier Li Keqiang greets Solomon Islands prime minister Manasseh Sogavare in Beijing in 2019.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Former Chinese premier Li Keqiang greets Solomon Islands prime minister Manasseh Sogavare in Beijing in 2019.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Thomas Peter/AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Pacific leaders have repeatedly stressed that they don\u2019t want to be drawn into a geostrategic contest between the US and China, and have resisted the notion of picking a side. They have also repeatedly said the climate crisis is their top security threat.</p> <p>However, China is increasingly active in seeking influence among Pacific island countries, including in security and policing. These efforts have prompted something of a reckoning in Australia, New Zealand and the US, which have all moved to step up their diplomatic efforts.</p> <p>Beijing\u2019s progress in the region was most sharply illustrated by the signing of a security agreement with Solomon Islands nearly a year ago.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/pacific-zip/giv-13425PMdXN9HXnSHN/\" data-alt=\"Pacific map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/pacific-zip/giv-13425PMdXN9HXnSHN/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>A leaked draft suggested that the agreement would allow China to respond to requests from police and armed forces to carry out a range of tasks such as maintaining social order and protecting \u201cthe safety of Chinese personnel and major projects in Solomon Islands\u201d.</p> <p>Australian officials had privately raised fears that the deal might pave the way for a future Chinese naval presence about 1,700km from the country\u2019s coast, although the prime minister of Solomon Islands, Manasseh Sogavare, has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/jul/14/solomon-islands-pm-rules-out-chinese-military-base-china-australia-security-partner-manasseh-sogavare\">repeatedly assured Australia and other countries</a> that there will never be a foreign military base in his country.</p> <p>China also <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/may/30/chinas-foreign-minister-to-meet-with-pacific-nations-amid-push-for-sweeping-regional-deal\">sought a sweeping regional security deal</a> with 10 Pacific countries last year, but this was rebuffed by Pacific leaders over concerns that Beijing was trying to bypass established structures such as the Pacific Islands Forum (PIF).</p> <p>Other countries have scrambled to respond to China\u2019s diplomatic pursuits. Since coming to power last year, Australia\u2019s Labor government has placed a priority on restoring trust with Pacific leaders. The foreign affairs minister, Penny Wong, and others have made a series of high-level visits stressing that security should be the responsibility of the immediate region (not China). Australia aims to finish negotiating a bilateral security agreement with Papua New Guinea by the end of April, having signed a deal with Vanuatu in December.</p> <p>The US has acknowledged its own need to step up in the region, which has seen it reopen its embassy in Solomon Islands in February, but western officials are under no illusions about the trends. Despite initial relief at the decision by Pacific island countries to defer China\u2019s sweeping security proposals, the Australian government believes this may be only a temporary reprieve. A senior government figure conceded last year: \u201cThings aren\u2019t going back to the way they were.\u201d</p> <p><em>Daniel Hurst</em> <em>in Canberra</em></p>", "wordcount": "1621", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-03-30T01:25:51Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T00:15:11Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/njmhd", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/e1c9cd91c6d02378c55e5ec36ed1269aa948a472/0_66_2000_1201/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "Global militarisation has increased rapidly over the course of the 21st century, with a clear recalibration towards the Asia Pacific. Between 2000 and 2021, annual military spending increased from $1.12trn (in 2020 dollar terms) to $2.11trn. In that time, the share of global defence spending in Asia and Oceania increased from 18% to 28%, according to data compiled by the Stockholm International Peace Research Institute. Growing concerns over China, whose defence spending has increased steadily for nearly three decades, has fuelled an arms race across the region, upending decades-long regional conventions and revealing several potential flashpoints. Taiwan The biggest concern about China\u2019s military expansion is how it ties in to plans to annex Taiwan. The risk of a Chinese invasion of Taiwan is the highest it\u2019s been in decades, but predictions of when it might happen vary. Recently a number of senior US military leaders have offered alarmingly imminent dates including 2023, 2025 and 2027. American assessments are crucial, given it\u2019s possible the US military will help defend Taiwan against Chinese attacks, but some analysts caution that these military figures are likely exaggerating to lobby for more funding. Sources say the public predictions have frustrated Taiwan\u2019s president, who is trying to ensure people are worried enough about the China threat that they support her policies, but not so much that they give up hope. However, the scale of China\u2019s military expansion offers analysts some clues to its ambitions for Taiwan. While still spending a smaller share of its GDP on defence than the US, China\u2019s defence budget will increase by 7.2% in 2023, taking it to the highest point it\u2019s ever been. The US Department of Defense estimates that China\u2019s real military spending could be up to twice as much as the officially reported figures. In response, the defence portion of the most recent US budget has prioritised ramping up the military presence in the Indo-Pacific. This month the US also approved the potential sale of $619m in new weapons to Taiwan. Just as important as the increased spending is how it affects the balance of military strength in the region. In 2000, China was the second-largest defence spender in the Indo-Pacific. By 2021 it was spending more on defence than the next 13 countries in the region combined. China has also taken great exception to a tripartite agreement between Australia, the US and the UK. The AU$368bn deal, known as Aukus, will see the US and UK help Australia acquire at least three nuclear-powered submarines over the next three decades. Aukus is widely seen as being aimed at countering China\u2019s military expansion, and has drawn mixed reactions. China accused the allies of deception, using loopholes in non-proliferation treaties, and \u201cwalking further and further down the path of error and danger\u201d. Australia\u2019s defence minister responded by saying, \u201cChina invests \u2026 in its own defence capabilities; we are doing that in respect of ours.\u201d Helen Davidson in Taipei and Amy Hawkins North Korea Having launched about 90 ballistic missiles and other weapons in 2022 \u2013 more than in any other year \u2013 North Korea appears intent on setting a new record this year. It has held 11 rounds of missile tests, some involving more than one weapon, so far in 2023, including two intercontinental ballistic missiles and, according to state media, strategic cruise missiles with a nuclear capability. In a sign that the regime in Pyongyang has no intention of relinquishing its weapons of mass destruction, it recently unveiled new, smaller nuclear warheads and vowed to produce more weapons-grade nuclear material. Kim Jong-un called for an \u201cexponential increase\u201d in the regime\u2019s nuclear arsenal at the end of last year. The North\u2019s provocations are directed towards its nemeses: the US and South Korea. As March drew to a close, forces from both countries engaged in joint maritime exercises that included nuclear-powered aircraft carrier the USS Nimitz, days after they ended their largest joint military exercises in five years. The allies insist the drills are purely defensive in nature, but Pyongyang condemns them as a rehearsal for an invasion. Rising tensions on the Korean peninsula have triggered a hardline response from South Korea\u2019s conservative president, Yoon Suk Yeol, who has warned that Seoul will not provide a \u201csingle penny\u201d to the North as long as it continues to develop nuclear weapons. More worryingly for those eager to dial down tensions, a serious debate is unfolding over whether Seoul should acquire its own nuclear deterrent \u2013 a move a majority of South Koreans support, according to polls. In Japan, concern over North Korea and an increasingly assertive China has boosted the case for steering the country further away from its postwar \u201cpacifism.\u201d Late last year, the government of prime minister Fumio Kishida announced Japan would double defence spending to 2% of GDP by 2027, abandoning its self-imposed cap of 1% of GDP. The increasingly unstable security environment in the Asia-Pacific has been the catalyst for a long-awaited thaw in relations between Japan and South Korea \u2013 both US allies with tens of thousands of American troops based on their territory. In March, Yoon and Kishida became the first South Korean and Japanese leaders to hold bilateral talks for 12 years. Their summit in Tokyo came amid attempts to resolve disputes connected to their bitter wartime legacy, and resulted in an agreement to resume reciprocal visits and a security dialogue after a five-year hiatus. Justin McCurry in Tokyo South China Sea The South China Sea is one of the most strategically and economically important waterways in the world. It is one of the world\u2019s busiest trading routes, rich in marine life and is thought to contain significant reserves of oil and natural gas. It\u2019s also highly contested. China claims almost all of the South China Sea, with its so-called \u201cnine-dash\u201d line marking the limit of the country\u2019s claim \u2013 though an international tribunal in The Hague has rejected this. The Philippines, as well as Vietnam, Malaysia, Brunei and Taiwan also have overlapping claims. The US, while not a claimant, considers the water crucial to its national interests. Under president Ferdinand Marcos, the Philippines has taken a much tougher stance on the dispute, with Manila accusing Chinese vessels of \u201caggressive actions\u201d in the South China Sea. Marcos has vowed the country \u201cwill not lose one inch\u201d of its territory, and has overseen a massive shift in foreign policy, strengthening defence ties with the US and its allies. In March the Philippines expanded US access to its military bases, enhancing Washington\u2019s footprint in the region, and enabling it to more easily monitor Chinese activity in the South China Sea near Taiwan. It has also strengthened military ties with Japan and Australia, two US allies. In contrast to neighbouring Southeast Asia countries that have expressed concerns over Aukus, which some nations fear could prompt an arms race that destabilises the region, the Philippines has supported the development. The approach taken by Marcos, who came to power last year, is a stark contrast to that of his predecessor, former president Rodrigo Duterte. Duterte had sought closer economic ties with Beijing and was reluctant to confront China over its activities in the South China sea \u2013 even as China constructed military bases on human-made islands, and Chinese vessels harassed, or rammed, Filippine boats. Rebecca Ratcliffe in Bangkok Pacific Islands Pacific leaders have repeatedly stressed that they don\u2019t want to be drawn into a geostrategic contest between the US and China, and have resisted the notion of picking a side. They have also repeatedly said the climate crisis is their top security threat. However, China is increasingly active in seeking influence among Pacific island countries, including in security and policing. These efforts have prompted something of a reckoning in Australia, New Zealand and the US, which have all moved to step up their diplomatic efforts. Beijing\u2019s progress in the region was most sharply illustrated by the signing of a security agreement with Solomon Islands nearly a year ago. A leaked draft suggested that the agreement would allow China to respond to requests from police and armed forces to carry out a range of tasks such as maintaining social order and protecting \u201cthe safety of Chinese personnel and major projects in Solomon Islands\u201d. Australian officials had privately raised fears that the deal might pave the way for a future Chinese naval presence about 1,700km from the country\u2019s coast, although the prime minister of Solomon Islands, Manasseh Sogavare, has repeatedly assured Australia and other countries that there will never be a foreign military base in his country. China also sought a sweeping regional security deal with 10 Pacific countries last year, but this was rebuffed by Pacific leaders over concerns that Beijing was trying to bypass established structures such as the Pacific Islands Forum (PIF). Other countries have scrambled to respond to China\u2019s diplomatic pursuits. Since coming to power last year, Australia\u2019s Labor government has placed a priority on restoring trust with Pacific leaders. The foreign affairs minister, Penny Wong, and others have made a series of high-level visits stressing that security should be the responsibility of the immediate region (not China). Australia aims to finish negotiating a bilateral security agreement with Papua New Guinea by the end of April, having signed a deal with Vanuatu in December. The US has acknowledged its own need to step up in the region, which has seen it reopen its embassy in Solomon Islands in February, but western officials are under no illusions about the trends. Despite initial relief at the decision by Pacific island countries to defer China\u2019s sweeping security proposals, the Australian government believes this may be only a temporary reprieve. Asia Pacific. Between 2000 and 2021, annual military spending increased from $1.12trn (in 2020 dollar terms) to $2.11trn. In that time, the share of global defence spending in Asia and Oceania increased from 18% to 28%, according to data compiled by the Stockholm International Peace Research Institute.</p> <p>Growing concerns over China, whose defence spending has increased steadily for nearly three decades, has fuelled an arms race across the region, upending decades-long regional conventions and revealing several potential flashpoints.</p> <h2><strong>Taiwan</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"ec74f5ad404396c2bfcb8a71049fe23f53fd5c69\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/ec74f5ad404396c2bfcb8a71049fe23f53fd5c69/138_440_5209_3125/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Chinese military helicopters take part in military drills following Nancy Pelosi\u2019s visit to Taiwan in 2022.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Chinese military helicopters take part in military drills following Nancy Pelosi\u2019s visit to Taiwan in 2022.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: H\u00e9ctor Retamal/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The biggest concern about China\u2019s military expansion is how it ties in to plans to annex Taiwan. The risk of a Chinese invasion of Taiwan is the highest it\u2019s been in decades, but predictions of when it might happen vary.</p> <p>Recently a number of senior US military leaders have offered <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/feb/02/us-general-gut-feeling-war-china-sparks-alarm-predictions\">alarmingly imminent dates</a> including 2023, 2025 and 2027. American assessments are crucial, given it\u2019s possible the US military will help defend Taiwan against Chinese attacks, but some analysts caution that these military figures are likely exaggerating to lobby for more funding. Sources say the public predictions have frustrated Taiwan\u2019s president, who is trying to ensure people are worried enough about the China threat that they support her policies, but not so much that they give up hope.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/tawian-zip/giv-13425IteVVK9WkF68/\" data-alt=\"Taiwan map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/tawian-zip/giv-13425IteVVK9WkF68/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>However, the scale of China\u2019s military expansion offers analysts some clues to its ambitions for Taiwan.</p> <p>While still spending a smaller share of its GDP on defence than the US, China\u2019s defence budget will increase by 7.2% in 2023, taking it to the highest point it\u2019s ever been. The US Department of Defense estimates that China\u2019s real military spending could be up to twice as much as the officially reported figures. In response, the defence portion of the most recent US budget has prioritised <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/10/biden-budget-includes-billions-for-pacific-islands-in-bid-to-out-compete-china\">ramping up the military presence</a> in the Indo-Pacific. This month the US also approved the potential sale of $619m in new weapons to Taiwan.</p> <p>Just as important as the increased spending is how it affects the balance of military strength in the region. In 2000, China was the second-largest defence spender in the Indo-Pacific. By 2021 it was spending more on defence than the next 13 countries in <a href=\"https://www.csis.org/analysis/what-does-china-really-spend-its-military#:~:text=In%20March%202023%2C%20China%20announced,1.45%20trillion%20(%24229.6%20billion).&amp;text=This%20continues%20a%20recent%20trend,in%20the%20upper%20single%20digits.\">the region combined</a>.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/charts/embed/mar/2023-03-29-01:20:00/embed.html\" data-alt=\"China defence spending chart\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/charts/embed/mar/2023-03-29-01:20:00/embed.html\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>China has also taken great exception to a tripartite agreement between Australia, the US and the UK. The AU$368bn deal, known as Aukus, will see the US and UK help Australia acquire at least three nuclear-powered submarines over the next three decades. Aukus is widely seen as being aimed at countering China\u2019s military expansion, and has drawn mixed reactions. China accused the allies of deception, using loopholes in non-proliferation treaties, and \u201cwalking further and further down the path of error and danger\u201d. Australia\u2019s defence minister responded by saying, \u201cChina invests \u2026 in its own defence capabilities; we are doing that in respect of ours.\u201d</p> <p><em>Helen Davidson in Taipei and Amy Hawkins</em></p> <h2><strong>North Korea</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"17f40379b67cb9986daf2294f97fc639daba485b\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/17f40379b67cb9986daf2294f97fc639daba485b/0_26_2674_1605/1000.jpg\" alt=\"North Korean leader Kim Jong-un inspects various types of warheads designed to be mounted on missiles or rocket launches.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">North Korean leader Kim Jong-un inspects various types of warheads designed to be mounted on missiles or rocket launches.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: \u671d\u9bae\u901a\u4fe1\u793e/AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Having launched about 90 ballistic missiles and other weapons in 2022 \u2013 more than in any other year \u2013 North Korea appears intent on setting a new record this year. It has held <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/19/north-korea-continues-run-of-weapons-tests-with-ballistic-missile-launch\">11 rounds of missile tests</a>, some involving more than one weapon, so far in 2023, including two intercontinental ballistic missiles and, according to state media, strategic cruise missiles with a nuclear capability.</p> <p>In a sign that the regime in Pyongyang has no intention of relinquishing its weapons of mass destruction, it recently unveiled new, smaller nuclear warheads and vowed to produce more weapons-grade nuclear material. Kim Jong-un called for an <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/jan/01/kim-jong-un-north-korea-exponentially-increase-nuclear-warhead-production\">\u201cexponential increase\u201d in the regime\u2019s nuclear arsenal</a> at the end of last year.</p> <p>The North\u2019s provocations are directed towards its nemeses: the US and South Korea. As March drew to a close, forces from both countries engaged in joint maritime exercises that included nuclear-powered aircraft carrier the USS Nimitz, days after they ended their largest joint military exercises in five years. The allies insist the drills are purely defensive in nature, but Pyongyang condemns them as a rehearsal for an invasion.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/n_korea-zip/giv-13425O9qdUjJwar8V/\" data-alt=\"Map of North Korea\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/n_korea-zip/giv-13425O9qdUjJwar8V/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Rising <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/aug/15/north-and-south-korea-tensions-relations-issue-conflict-division-explained-in-30-seconds\">tensions on the Korean peninsula</a> have triggered a hardline response from South Korea\u2019s conservative president, Yoon Suk Yeol, who has warned that Seoul will not provide a \u201csingle penny\u201d to the North as long as it continues to develop nuclear weapons. More worryingly for those eager to dial down tensions, a serious debate is unfolding over whether Seoul should acquire its own nuclear deterrent \u2013 a move a majority of South Koreans support, according to polls.</p> <p>In Japan, concern over North Korea and an increasingly assertive China has boosted the case for steering the country further away from its postwar \u201cpacifism.\u201d Late last year, the government of prime minister Fumio Kishida announced Japan would <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/dec/16/japan-approves-biggest-military-buildup-since-second-world-war-amid-china-fears\">double defence spending to 2% of GDP</a> by 2027, abandoning its self-imposed cap of 1% of GDP.</p> <p>The increasingly unstable security environment in the Asia-Pacific has been the catalyst for a long-awaited thaw in relations between Japan and South Korea \u2013 both US allies with tens of thousands of American troops based on their territory.</p> <p>In March, Yoon and Kishida became the first South Korean and Japanese leaders <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/16/yoon-arrives-in-japan-for-historic-talks-with-kishida-and-beloved-omurice\">to hold bilateral talks for 12 years</a>. Their summit in Tokyo came amid attempts to resolve disputes connected to their bitter wartime legacy, and resulted in an agreement to resume reciprocal visits and a security dialogue after a five-year hiatus.</p> <p><em>Justin McCurry in Tokyo</em></p> <h2><strong>South China Sea</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"f59f55677b28705fbef57c2fd74334af29d784e0\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f59f55677b28705fbef57c2fd74334af29d784e0/0_7_6000_3600/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Philippine troops gather as president Ferdinand Marcos Jr. announces four new military bases, including in a province facing the South China Sea.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Philippine troops gather as president Ferdinand Marcos Jr. announces four new military bases, including in a province facing the South China Sea.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Ezra Acayan/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The South China Sea is one of the most strategically and economically important waterways in the world. It is one of the world\u2019s busiest trading routes, rich in marine life and is thought to contain significant reserves of oil and natural gas. It\u2019s also highly contested.</p> <p>China claims almost all of the South China Sea, with its so-called \u201cnine-dash\u201d line marking the limit of the country\u2019s claim \u2013 though an<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/jul/12/philippines-wins-south-china-sea-case-against-china\"> international tribunal in The Hague has rejected this</a>. The Philippines, as well as Vietnam, Malaysia, Brunei and Taiwan also have overlapping claims. The US, while not a claimant, considers the water crucial to its national interests.</p> <p>Under president Ferdinand Marcos, the Philippines has taken a much tougher stance on the dispute, with Manila accusing Chinese vessels of \u201caggressive actions\u201d in the South China Sea.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/s_china_sea-zip/giv-13425sGxq1YFye1KF/\" data-alt=\"South China Sea map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/s_china_sea-zip/giv-13425sGxq1YFye1KF/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>Marcos has vowed the country \u201cwill not lose one inch\u201d of its territory, and has overseen a massive shift in foreign policy, strengthening defence ties with the US and its allies.</p> <p>In March the Philippines <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/mar/18/the-tiny-philippine-island-on-the-frontline-of-the-us-china-battle-for-supremacy\">expanded US access to its military bases</a>, enhancing Washington\u2019s footprint in the region, and enabling it to more easily monitor Chinese activity in the South China Sea near Taiwan. It has also strengthened military ties with Japan and Australia, two US allies.</p> <p>In contrast to neighbouring Southeast Asia countries that have expressed concerns over Aukus, which some nations fear could prompt an arms race that destabilises the region, the Philippines has supported the development.</p> <p>The approach taken by Marcos, who came to power last year, is a stark contrast to that of his predecessor, former president Rodrigo Duterte. Duterte had sought closer economic ties with Beijing and was reluctant to confront China over its activities in the South China sea \u2013 even as China constructed military bases on human-made islands, and Chinese vessels harassed, or rammed, Filippine boats.</p> <p><em>Rebecca Ratcliffe in Bangkok</em></p> <h2><strong>Pacific Islands</strong></h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"fa289ea88434fe42838636b225c0af17de597362\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/fa289ea88434fe42838636b225c0af17de597362/0_332_5000_3001/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Former Chinese premier Li Keqiang greets Solomon Islands prime minister Manasseh Sogavare in Beijing in 2019.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Former Chinese premier Li Keqiang greets Solomon Islands prime minister Manasseh Sogavare in Beijing in 2019.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Thomas Peter/AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Pacific leaders have repeatedly stressed that they don\u2019t want to be drawn into a geostrategic contest between the US and China, and have resisted the notion of picking a side. They have also repeatedly said the climate crisis is their top security threat.</p> <p>However, China is increasingly active in seeking influence among Pacific island countries, including in security and policing. These efforts have prompted something of a reckoning in Australia, New Zealand and the US, which have all moved to step up their diplomatic efforts.</p> <p>Beijing\u2019s progress in the region was most sharply illustrated by the signing of a security agreement with Solomon Islands nearly a year ago.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive element--supporting\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/pacific-zip/giv-13425PMdXN9HXnSHN/\" data-alt=\"Pacific map\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2023/03/pacific-zip/giv-13425PMdXN9HXnSHN/\">Interactive</a> </figure>  <p>A leaked draft suggested that the agreement would allow China to respond to requests from police and armed forces to carry out a range of tasks such as maintaining social order and protecting \u201cthe safety of Chinese personnel and major projects in Solomon Islands\u201d.</p> <p>Australian officials had privately raised fears that the deal might pave the way for a future Chinese naval presence about 1,700km from the country\u2019s coast, although the prime minister of Solomon Islands, Manasseh Sogavare, has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/jul/14/solomon-islands-pm-rules-out-chinese-military-base-china-australia-security-partner-manasseh-sogavare\">repeatedly assured Australia and other countries</a> that there will never be a foreign military base in his country.</p> <p>China also <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/may/30/chinas-foreign-minister-to-meet-with-pacific-nations-amid-push-for-sweeping-regional-deal\">sought a sweeping regional security deal</a> with 10 Pacific countries last year, but this was rebuffed by Pacific leaders over concerns that Beijing was trying to bypass established structures such as the Pacific Islands Forum (PIF).</p> <p>Other countries have scrambled to respond to China\u2019s diplomatic pursuits. Since coming to power last year, Australia\u2019s Labor government has placed a priority on restoring trust with Pacific leaders. The foreign affairs minister, Penny Wong, and others have made a series of high-level visits stressing that security should be the responsibility of the immediate region (not China). Australia aims to finish negotiating a bilateral security agreement with Papua New Guinea by the end of April, having signed a deal with Vanuatu in December.</p> <p>The US has acknowledged its own need to step up in the region, which has seen it reopen its embassy in Solomon Islands in February, but western officials are under no illusions about the trends. Despite initial relief at the decision by Pacific island countries to defer China\u2019s sweeping security proposals, the Australian government believes this may be only a temporary reprieve. A senior government figure conceded last year: \u201cThings aren\u2019t going back to the way they were.\u201d</p> <p><em>Daniel Hurst</em> <em>in Canberra</em></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Global militarisation has increased rapidly over the course of the 21st century, with a clear recalibration towards the Asia Pacific. In a sign that the regime in Pyongyang has no intention of relinquishing its weapons of mass destruction, it recently unveiled new, smaller nuclear warheads and vowed to produce more weapons-grade nuclear material. Kim Jong-un called for an \u201cexponential increase\u201d in the regime\u2019s nuclear arsenal at the end of last year. The North\u2019s provocations are directed towards its nemeses: the US and South Korea. As March drew to a close, forces from both countries engaged in joint maritime exercises that included nuclear-powered aircraft carrier the USS Nimitz, days after they ended their largest joint military exercises in five years. The allies insist the drills are purely defensive in nature, but Pyongyang condemns them as a rehearsal for an invasion.\nRising tensions on the Korean peninsula have triggered a hardline response from South Korea\u2019s conservative president, Yoon Suk Yeol, who has warned that Seoul will not provide a \u201csingle penny\u201d to the North as long as it continues to develop nuclear weapons. The increasingly unstable security environment in the Asia-Pacific has been the catalyst for a long-awaited thaw in relations between Japan and South Korea \u2013 both US allies with tens of thousands of American troops based on their territory. In March, Yoon and Kishida became the first South Korean and Japanese leaders to hold bilateral talks for 12 years. Their summit in Tokyo came amid attempts to resolve disputes connected to their bitter wartime legacy, and resulted in an agreement to resume reciprocal visits and a security dialogue after a five-year hiatus. Justin McCurry in Tokyo South China Sea\nThe South China Sea is one of the most strategically and economically important waterways in the world. It is one of the world\u2019s busiest trading routes, rich in marine life and is thought to contain significant reserves of oil and natural gas. It\u2019s also highly contested. It has also strengthened military ties with Japan and Australia, two US allies. Rebecca Ratcliffe in Bangkok Pacific Islands\nPacific leaders have repeatedly stressed that they don\u2019t want to be drawn into a geostrategic contest between the US and China, and have resisted the notion of picking a side. They have also repeatedly said the climate crisis is their top security threat. However, China is increasingly active in seeking influence among Pacific island countries, including in security and policing. These efforts have prompted something of a reckoning in Australia, New Zealand and the US, which have all moved to step up their diplomatic efforts. Beijing\u2019s progress in the region was most sharply illustrated by the signing of a security agreement with Solomon Islands nearly a year ago.\nA leaked draft suggested that the agreement would allow China to respond to requests from police and armed forces to carry out a range of tasks such as maintaining social order and protecting \u201cthe safety of Chinese personnel and major projects in Solomon Islands\u201d. Australian officials had privately raised fears that the deal might pave the way for a future Chinese naval presence about 1,700km from the country\u2019s coast, although the prime minister of Solomon Islands, Manasseh Sogavare, has repeatedly assured Australia and other countries that there will never be a foreign military base in his country. China also sought a sweeping regional security deal with 10 Pacific countries last year, but this was rebuffed by Pacific leaders over concerns that Beijing was trying to bypass established structures such as the Pacific Islands Forum (PIF). Other countries have scrambled to respond to China\u2019s diplomatic pursuits. Since coming to power last year, Australia\u2019s Labor government has placed a priority on restoring trust with Pacific leaders. The foreign affairs minister, Penny Wong, and others have made a series of high-level visits stressing that security should be the responsibility of the immediate region (not China). Australia aims to finish negotiating a bilateral security agreement with Papua New Guinea by the end of April, having signed a deal with Vanuatu in December. The US has acknowledged its own need to step up in the region, which has seen it reopen its embassy in Solomon Islands in February, but western officials are under no illusions about the trends. Despite initial relief at the decision by Pacific island countries to defer China\u2019s sweeping security proposals, the Australian government believes this may be only a temporary reprieve. href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/30/australias-powers-to-detain-terrorist-offenders-to-prevent-future-must-be-abolished-watchdog-says\">damning report</a> by Australia\u2019s national security law watchdog recommended scrapping continuing detention orders, which allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned for three years on the basis of predicted crimes rather than for any crime they have committed.</p> <p>In the report the independent national security legislation monitor, Grant Donaldson, said he doubted \u201cthat anyone knows whether [the powers] have made us safer\u201d and said CDOs were completely disproportionate to any terror threat.</p> <p>The use of such preventive imprisonment powers made Australia an outlier among western nations, he said.</p> <p>\u201cAustralia leads the world in making laws of a kind discussed in this report,\u201d he said at the outset of his report.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>The Law Council of Australia on Monday endorsed Donaldson\u2019s findings and called on the federal government to urgently consider reforms to abolish CDOs.</p> <p>\u201cThe Law Council\u2019s longstanding position is that the CDO regime is not a necessary or proportionate response to the threat of terrorism,\u201d said its president, Luke Murphy.</p> <p>\u201cDetention should not be available based on a prediction of a person\u2019s future risk, which is a fraught exercise in the absence of an empirically validated risk assessment methodology.\u201d</p> <p>The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he would \u201cconsider all of the recommendations\u201d raised in Donaldson\u2019s report.</p> <p>But he also said the government would \u201calways put the safety and security of Australians first\u201d.</p> <p>Asked whether the powers had made Australians safer, Dreyfus said: \u201cI\u2019ve only just received the report and of course have tabled it. We will be considering the report in due course.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/24/australian-government-to-restore-powers-to-strip-citizenship-from-terrorism-suspects\">Australian government to restore powers to strip citizenship from terrorism suspects </a> </p> </aside>  <p>The Australian Human Rights Commission has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/law/2022/jun/22/australias-powers-to-detain-terrorist-offenders-after-jail-term-without-rehabilitation-under-review\">repeatedly expressed concern</a> about the use of CDOs. In its submission to Donaldson, the commission said the regime \u201cimposes very severe restrictions on liberty outside the parameters of the ordinary criminal justice system\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIn order to justify the extraordinary nature of the regime, there must be a substantial risk that the anticipated harm will in fact occur,\u201d the commission said. \u201cThe commission considers that the current threshold does not strike an appropriate balance by permitting orders for continuing detention to be imposed where the risk of future harm is slight.\u201d</p> <p>Donaldson\u2019s report also criticised the former government\u2019s handling of a sensitive report, produced by academic Emily Corner, questioning the reliability of the tool used to determine a person\u2019s likelihood of reoffending.</p> <p>The government did not release the report and did not provide it to the subjects of CDOs, a fact <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/15/terrorists-lawyers-tell-supreme-court-government-may-have-conspired-to-pervert-the-course-of-justice\">revealed last year by Guardian Australia</a>.</p> <p>Donaldson described the handling of the report by the department as \u201cshocking\u201d and something of \u201cvery great concern\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIt is shocking that orders have been made, and are being sought, with parties unaware that Dr Corner\u2019s report exists,\u201d he said. \u201cAs a result of this report, those who are the subject of applications for post-sentence orders, and courts dealing with these applications, will at least now be aware of its existence.\u201d</p> <p>Guardian Australia has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/19/a-wicked-problem-how-to-deal-with-terrorists-if-they-are-allegedly-still-a-risk-after-serving-their-sentence\">previously revealed</a> that lawyers for the convicted terrorist Abdul Nacer Benbrika say the government may have conspired to pervert the course of justice when it did not disclose the report.</p> <p>Donaldson\u2019s report also laid bare stark funding problems for the targets of CDO applications, which compromised their ability to be legally represented. Donaldson found virtually all defendants \u201cwill not be able to afford representation\u201d and would be unlikely to be able to get support from legal aid bodies in various jurisdictions within the necessary timeframe.</p> <p>\u201cThe commonwealth has the obligation to fund defendants confronting these applications and must fund them timeously and adequately,\u201d Donaldson found.</p> <p>The Law Council said it \u201cstrongly supports\u201d the recommendation that the commonwealth be obliged to fund the legal costs of defendants.</p> <p>\u201cIt is essential that defendants have, or be provided with, the capacity to respond to these applications in a manner consistent with their seriousness,\u201d Murphy said.</p> <p>The law also allows for post-sentence supervision of terrorist offenders through extended supervision orders. Donaldson recommended that the ESO powers remain but be reformed to ensure they focus primarily on rehabilitation and reintegration.</p> <p>The Law Council agreed in principle that ESOs should remain but backed the call for reform, saying there must be an effort to \u201cimprove proportionality and better achieve the objectives of rehabilitation and reintegration of the defendant into the community\u201d.</p>", "wordcount": "752", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-04T00:00:31Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-04T00:55:00Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nkm74", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/e32d4dbe38dc8b4e3ba967fd5b5e8018793a734b/0_162_3870_2322/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "Australia\u2019s peak lawyers body has urged the government to abolish \u201cfraught\u201d powers that allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned so as to prevent possible crimes being committed in the future. Last week a damning report by Australia\u2019s national security law watchdog recommended scrapping continuing detention orders, which allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned for three years on the basis of predicted crimes rather than for any crime they have committed. In the report the independent national security legislation monitor, Grant Donaldson, said he doubted \u201cthat anyone knows whether [the powers] have made us safer\u201d and said CDOs were completely disproportionate to any terror threat. The use of such preventive imprisonment powers made Australia an outlier among western nations, he said. \u201cAustralia leads the world in making laws of a kind discussed in this report,\u201d he said at the outset of his report. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup The Law Council of Australia on Monday endorsed Donaldson\u2019s findings and called on the federal government to urgently consider reforms to abolish CDOs. \u201cThe Law Council\u2019s longstanding position is that the CDO regime is not a necessary or proportionate response to the threat of terrorism,\u201d said its president, Luke Murphy. \u201cDetention should not be available based on a prediction of a person\u2019s future risk, which is a fraught exercise in the absence of an empirically validated risk assessment methodology.\u201d The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he would \u201cconsider all of the recommendations\u201d raised in Donaldson\u2019s report. But he also said the government would \u201calways put the safety and security of Australians first\u201d. Asked whether the powers had made Australians safer, Dreyfus said: \u201cI\u2019ve only just received the report and of course have tabled it. We will be considering the report in due course.\u201d The Australian Human Rights Commission has repeatedly expressed concern about the use of CDOs. In its submission to Donaldson, the commission said the regime \u201cimposes very severe restrictions on liberty outside the parameters of the ordinary criminal justice system\u201d. \u201cIn order to justify the extraordinary nature of the regime, there must be a substantial risk that the anticipated harm will in fact occur,\u201d the commission said. \u201cThe commission considers that the current threshold does not strike an appropriate balance by permitting orders for continuing detention to be imposed where the risk of future harm is slight.\u201d Donaldson\u2019s report also criticised the former government\u2019s handling of a sensitive report, produced by academic Emily Corner, questioning the reliability of the tool used to determine a person\u2019s likelihood of reoffending. The government did not release the report and did not provide it to the subjects of CDOs, a fact revealed last year by Guardian Australia. Donaldson described the handling of the report by the department as \u201cshocking\u201d and something of \u201cvery great concern\u201d. \u201cIt is shocking that orders have been made, and are being sought, with parties unaware that Dr Corner\u2019s report exists,\u201d he said. \u201cAs a result of this report, those who are the subject of applications for post-sentence orders, and courts dealing with these applications, will at least now be aware of its existence.\u201d Guardian Australia has previously revealed that lawyers for the convicted terrorist Abdul Nacer Benbrika say the government may have conspired to pervert the course of justice when it did not disclose the report. Donaldson\u2019s report also laid bare stark funding problems for the targets of CDO applications, which compromised their ability to be legally represented. Donaldson found virtually all defendants \u201cwill not be able to afford representation\u201d and would be unlikely to be able to get support from legal aid bodies in various jurisdictions within the necessary timeframe. \u201cThe commonwealth has the obligation to fund defendants confronting these applications and must fund them timeously and adequately,\u201d Donaldson found. The Law Council said it \u201cstrongly supports\u201d the recommendation that the commonwealth be obliged to fund the legal costs of defendants. \u201cIt is essential that defendants have, or be provided with, the capacity to respond to these applications in a manner consistent with their seriousness,\u201d Murphy said. The law also allows for post-sentence supervision of terrorist offenders through extended supervision orders. Donaldson recommended that the ESO powers remain but be reformed to ensure they focus primarily on rehabilitation and reintegration. The Law Council agreed in principle that ESOs should remain but backed the call for reform, saying there must be an effort to \u201cimprove proportionality and better achieve the objectives of href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/30/australias-powers-to-detain-terrorist-offenders-to-prevent-future-must-be-abolished-watchdog-says\">damning report</a> by Australia\u2019s national security law watchdog recommended scrapping continuing detention orders, which allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned for three years on the basis of predicted crimes rather than for any crime they have committed.</p> <p>In the report the independent national security legislation monitor, Grant Donaldson, said he doubted \u201cthat anyone knows whether [the powers] have made us safer\u201d and said CDOs were completely disproportionate to any terror threat.</p> <p>The use of such preventive imprisonment powers made Australia an outlier among western nations, he said.</p> <p>\u201cAustralia leads the world in making laws of a kind discussed in this report,\u201d he said at the outset of his report.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>The Law Council of Australia on Monday endorsed Donaldson\u2019s findings and called on the federal government to urgently consider reforms to abolish CDOs.</p> <p>\u201cThe Law Council\u2019s longstanding position is that the CDO regime is not a necessary or proportionate response to the threat of terrorism,\u201d said its president, Luke Murphy.</p> <p>\u201cDetention should not be available based on a prediction of a person\u2019s future risk, which is a fraught exercise in the absence of an empirically validated risk assessment methodology.\u201d</p> <p>The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he would \u201cconsider all of the recommendations\u201d raised in Donaldson\u2019s report.</p> <p>But he also said the government would \u201calways put the safety and security of Australians first\u201d.</p> <p>Asked whether the powers had made Australians safer, Dreyfus said: \u201cI\u2019ve only just received the report and of course have tabled it. We will be considering the report in due course.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/24/australian-government-to-restore-powers-to-strip-citizenship-from-terrorism-suspects\">Australian government to restore powers to strip citizenship from terrorism suspects </a> </p> </aside>  <p>The Australian Human Rights Commission has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/law/2022/jun/22/australias-powers-to-detain-terrorist-offenders-after-jail-term-without-rehabilitation-under-review\">repeatedly expressed concern</a> about the use of CDOs. In its submission to Donaldson, the commission said the regime \u201cimposes very severe restrictions on liberty outside the parameters of the ordinary criminal justice system\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIn order to justify the extraordinary nature of the regime, there must be a substantial risk that the anticipated harm will in fact occur,\u201d the commission said. \u201cThe commission considers that the current threshold does not strike an appropriate balance by permitting orders for continuing detention to be imposed where the risk of future harm is slight.\u201d</p> <p>Donaldson\u2019s report also criticised the former government\u2019s handling of a sensitive report, produced by academic Emily Corner, questioning the reliability of the tool used to determine a person\u2019s likelihood of reoffending.</p> <p>The government did not release the report and did not provide it to the subjects of CDOs, a fact <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/dec/15/terrorists-lawyers-tell-supreme-court-government-may-have-conspired-to-pervert-the-course-of-justice\">revealed last year by Guardian Australia</a>.</p> <p>Donaldson described the handling of the report by the department as \u201cshocking\u201d and something of \u201cvery great concern\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cIt is shocking that orders have been made, and are being sought, with parties unaware that Dr Corner\u2019s report exists,\u201d he said. \u201cAs a result of this report, those who are the subject of applications for post-sentence orders, and courts dealing with these applications, will at least now be aware of its existence.\u201d</p> <p>Guardian Australia has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/nov/19/a-wicked-problem-how-to-deal-with-terrorists-if-they-are-allegedly-still-a-risk-after-serving-their-sentence\">previously revealed</a> that lawyers for the convicted terrorist Abdul Nacer Benbrika say the government may have conspired to pervert the course of justice when it did not disclose the report.</p> <p>Donaldson\u2019s report also laid bare stark funding problems for the targets of CDO applications, which compromised their ability to be legally represented. Donaldson found virtually all defendants \u201cwill not be able to afford representation\u201d and would be unlikely to be able to get support from legal aid bodies in various jurisdictions within the necessary timeframe.</p> <p>\u201cThe commonwealth has the obligation to fund defendants confronting these applications and must fund them timeously and adequately,\u201d Donaldson found.</p> <p>The Law Council said it \u201cstrongly supports\u201d the recommendation that the commonwealth be obliged to fund the legal costs of defendants.</p> <p>\u201cIt is essential that defendants have, or be provided with, the capacity to respond to these applications in a manner consistent with their seriousness,\u201d Murphy said.</p> <p>The law also allows for post-sentence supervision of terrorist offenders through extended supervision orders. Donaldson recommended that the ESO powers remain but be reformed to ensure they focus primarily on rehabilitation and reintegration.</p> <p>The Law Council agreed in principle that ESOs should remain but backed the call for reform, saying there must be an effort to \u201cimprove proportionality and better achieve the objectives of rehabilitation and reintegration of the defendant into the community\u201d.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Australia\u2019s peak lawyers body has urged the government to abolish \u201cfraught\u201d powers that allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned so as to prevent possible crimes being committed in the future. Last week a damning report by Australia\u2019s national security law watchdog recommended scrapping continuing detention orders, which allow terrorist offenders to be imprisoned for three years on the basis of predicted crimes rather than for any crime they have committed. In the report the independent national security legislation monitor, Grant Donaldson, said he doubted \u201cthat anyone knows whether [the powers] have made us safer\u201d and said CDOs were completely disproportionate to any terror threat. The use of such preventive imprisonment powers made Australia an outlier among western nations, he said. \u201cAustralia leads the world in making laws of a kind discussed in this report,\u201d he said at the outset of his report. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup The Law Council of Australia on Monday endorsed Donaldson\u2019s findings and called on the federal government to urgently consider reforms to abolish CDOs. \u201cThe Law Council\u2019s longstanding position is that the CDO regime is not a necessary or proportionate response to the threat of terrorism,\u201d said its president, Luke Murphy. \u201cDetention should not be available based on a prediction of a person\u2019s future risk, which is a fraught exercise in the absence of an empirically validated risk assessment methodology.\u201d The attorney general, Mark Dreyfus, said he would \u201cconsider all of the recommendations\u201d raised in Donaldson\u2019s report. But he also said the government would \u201calways put the safety and security of Australians first\u201d. Asked whether the powers had made Australians safer, Dreyfus said: \u201cI\u2019ve only just received the report and of course have tabled it. We will be considering the report in due course.\u201d\nThe Australian Human Rights Commission has repeatedly expressed concern about the use of CDOs. In its submission to Donaldson, the commission said the regime \u201cimposes very severe restrictions on liberty outside the parameters of the ordinary criminal justice system\u201d. \u201cIn order to justify the extraordinary nature of the regime, there must be a substantial risk that the anticipated harm will in fact occur,\u201d the commission said. \u201cThe commission considers that the current threshold does not strike an appropriate balance by permitting orders for continuing detention to be imposed where the risk of future harm is slight.\u201d Donaldson\u2019s report also criticised the former government\u2019s handling of a sensitive report, produced by academic Emily Corner, questioning the reliability of the tool used to determine a person\u2019s likelihood of reoffending. The government did not release the report and did not provide it to the subjects of CDOs, a fact revealed last year by Guardian Australia. Donaldson described the handling of the report by the department as \u201cshocking\u201d and something of \u201cvery great concern\u201d. \u201cIt is shocking that orders have been made, and are being sought, with parties unaware that Dr Corner\u2019s report exists,\u201d he said. \u201cAs a result of this report, those who are the subject of applications for post-sentence orders, and courts dealing with these applications, will at least now be aware of its existence.\u201d Guardian Australia has previously revealed that lawyers for the convicted terrorist Abdul Nacer Benbrika say the government may have conspired to pervert the course of justice when it did not disclose the report. Donaldson\u2019s report also laid bare stark funding problems for the targets of CDO applications, which compromised their ability to be legally represented. Donaldson found virtually all defendants \u201cwill not be able to afford representation\u201d and would be unlikely to be able to get support from legal aid bodies in various jurisdictions within the necessary timeframe. \u201cThe commonwealth has the obligation to fund defendants confronting these applications and must fund them timeously and adequately,\u201d Donaldson found. The Law Council said it \u201cstrongly supports\u201d the recommendation that the commonwealth be obliged to fund the legal costs of defendants. \u201cIt is essential that defendants have, or be provided with, the capacity to respond to these applications in a manner consistent with their seriousness,\u201d Murphy said. The law also allows for post-sentence supervision of terrorist offenders through extended supervision orders. Donaldson recommended that the ESO powers remain but be reformed to ensure they focus primarily on rehabilitation and reintegration. The Law Council agreed in principle that ESOs should remain but backed the call for reform, saying there must be an effort to \u201cimprove proportionality and better achieve the objectives of
